Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Lesson 01 · The Azure Administrator Toolset

Master the tools, interfaces, and management hierarchy you'll use every day as an Azure administrator.

  • Identify the Azure administrator role and core responsibilities
  • Navigate the Azure portal, customize dashboards, and launch Cloud Shell
  • Choose between portal, Azure CLI, Az PowerShell, and ARM/Bicep templates for a given task
  • Describe the management hierarchy: management groups → subscriptions → resource groups → resources
  • Move resources between resource groups and subscriptions — validation rules, GUID retention, and constraints
This lesson is deliberately tool-focused — the goal is to make every attendee comfortable before they hit the labs. Emphasise that the portal is great for exploration, but the exam tests whether candidates know WHEN to reach for CLI, PowerShell, or templates. The management hierarchy section directly feeds the governance content in Module 1.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

The Azure Administrator Role

Azure administrators implement, monitor, and maintain Azure infrastructure — translating business requirements into resilient cloud solutions.

🏗️

Implement

Deploy virtual networks, storage accounts, VMs, and managed identities to spec

📊

Monitor

Set up alerts, review diagnostics logs, and interpret Azure Monitor Insights

🔒

Secure & Govern

Assign RBAC roles, apply Azure Policy, and enforce tagging through management groups

💰

Optimize

Right-size resources, configure autoscale, manage reservations, and review Cost Management

🎯 Exam tip: the AZ-104 tests hands-on judgment — expect scenario questions about choosing the right action, not just naming a service.
Ground the audience in what "Azure administrator" means day-to-day. It's broader than just clicking around the portal — it covers the full lifecycle from planning through decommission. Stress that the exam is scenario-driven and rewards practical experience over memorisation. Ask who in the room currently manages on-premises infrastructure — they'll notice many concepts map 1:1.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Azure Portal, Dashboards & Cloud Shell

Portal Essentials

  • Pin resources to dashboards — one dashboard per team/workload
  • Star services in Favorites for left-nav quick access
  • Use the global search bar (top) to jump to any resource by name or type
  • Notifications bell — all async operations (deployments, role assignments) report here
  • Set Default subscription filter to avoid cross-subscription confusion

Cloud Shell

  • Browser-based shell — no local install needed; authenticated automatically
  • Choose Bash (Azure CLI) or PowerShell (Az module) per session
  • Backed by a 5 GB Azure Files share — your scripts persist across sessions
  • Idle timeout: 20 minutes — session terminates; storage is retained
  • Free compute — you pay only for the storage account (~$0.07/GB/month)
🔧 Lab tip: if Cloud Shell shows "No storage mounted", click Show advanced settings and create the storage account in a region close to you — first-time setup takes ~60 seconds.
Spend a moment showing the Cloud Shell toggle between Bash and PowerShell modes — many candidates don't realise the same persistent storage backs both modes. The 20-minute idle timeout catches people out in labs; warn them to run a keep-alive command if they step away. The exam does not test portal navigation paths directly, but labs assume portal familiarity.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Choosing the Right Tool

Each interface has a sweet spot — picking the wrong one costs time in production and exam points on test day.

Tool Best for ✅ Strengths ❌ Avoid when
Portal One-off tasks, exploration, visualising topology Guided wizards, inline docs, no toolchain needed Repetitive tasks, automation, multi-environment deploys
Azure CLI Scripting, CI/CD pipelines, cross-platform teams Bash-native, lightweight, great JSON output + --query Windows-only shops that standardise on Az PowerShell
Az PowerShell Windows automation, existing PS runbooks, Azure Automation Object pipeline, strong .NET integration, familiar to Windows admins Linux/Mac-first environments or when CLI coverage is sufficient
ARM / Bicep Repeatable, version-controlled, multi-environment IaC Idempotent, full resource coverage, source-control friendly Ad-hoc changes or when speed of exploration matters more than repeatability
🎯 Exam tip: questions about deploying the same configuration to multiple environments always point to ARM templates or Bicep — not the portal, not CLI scripts.
This comparison table is a direct exam topic. The most common distractor is CLI or PowerShell for multi-environment deployments — scripts can drift between environments. Templates are declarative and idempotent, which is why the exam favours them for anything repeatable. Bicep compiles to ARM, so both answers may appear correct; focus on "IaC" as the keyword signal in the question stem.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Management Hierarchy & Moving Resources

The Four Levels

  • Management Groups — nest up to 6 levels; policy and RBAC inherit downward to all subscriptions below
  • Subscriptions — billing boundary and scale unit; hard limits (e.g. 980 resource groups per subscription)
  • Resource Groups — deployment and lifecycle container; every resource belongs to exactly one RG
  • Resources — individual service instances (VM, storage account, VNet, etc.)
🎯 Exam tip: policies and RBAC assigned at a management group apply to all descendant subscriptions and resource groups — the exam tests this inheritance frequently.

Moving Resources

  • Move between resource groups: same subscription; resource GUID is retained
  • Move between subscriptions: source and target must be in the same Microsoft Entra tenant
  • Before moving, Azure runs a validation check — not all resource types are movable (e.g. classic resources, some networking resources)
  • Source RG and target RG are locked during the move — no write or delete operations
  • Associated resources (e.g. VNet of a VM) must be moved together or dependencies break
Gotcha: moving a resource does not change its resource ID (GUID) — but the ARM resource path changes, which can break hardcoded references in scripts or connection strings.
The hierarchy slide sets up the entire governance module — plant the seed that "scope" matters for every RBAC and policy assignment. On moving resources, the lock behaviour catches candidates out: the move window can take several minutes and all RG operations are blocked. The GUID retention point is subtle — resource ID path changes even though the underlying GUID doesn't, so scripts referencing the old path will break. Use a concrete example: moving a VM from rg-dev to rg-prod in the same subscription.

Azure management hierarchy

Azure management hierarchy: management group → subscription → resource group → resources, with governance inheritance arrows

Knowledge Check

Lesson 01 · Scenario

A company operates three environments — Development, Staging, and Production — each in its own Azure subscription. The infrastructure team must deploy an identical set of virtual networks, subnets, and network security groups to all three environments on a weekly release cadence. Configuration drift between environments has caused two production incidents in the past month. What should you do?

A Write an Azure CLI Bash script that creates the resources and run it manually against each subscription
B Use the Azure portal to create the resources in each subscription, following a documented checklist
C Author a Bicep template with environment-specific parameter files and deploy it via a CI/CD pipeline targeting each subscription
D Use Az PowerShell scripts stored in Azure Automation and trigger them on a schedule for each subscription
✓ C is correct — Bicep (or ARM) templates are declarative and idempotent; parameter files encode per-environment differences while the template guarantees configuration consistency across all three subscriptions. Deploying through a CI/CD pipeline eliminates manual steps and provides an audit trail.
  • A — CLI scripts are imperative and non-idempotent; they drift when partially re-run and require manual sequencing per subscription, which is exactly what caused the incidents
  • B — the portal is the highest-risk option for repeatable multi-environment work: it is manual, error-prone, and leaves no version-controlled record of the intended state
  • D — Az PowerShell scripts share the same idempotency problem as CLI scripts; Azure Automation adds scheduling but does not solve configuration drift across environments the way a declarative template does
Give attendees 45 seconds before revealing the answer — this scenario is representative of real AZ-104 exam questions. The key discriminator is "repeatable multi-environment" plus "drift" — those two signals together always point to IaC templates. Emphasise that both CLI and PowerShell scripts can work for one-off tasks but are not the right tool when idempotency and consistency across environments are the requirements.
Module 00 · Lesson 02

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Azure CLI & Azure PowerShell Essentials

  • Master Azure CLI syntax: az <group> [<subgroup>] <verb> structure and output formats
  • Use JMESPath --query to filter, project, and transform CLI output
  • Apply Az PowerShell Verb-AzNoun patterns and context management
  • Write idempotent scripts — safe for re-runs with check-before-create patterns
  • Choose confidently between Azure CLI and Az PowerShell for any scenario
This lesson covers the two primary command-line automation tools every Azure Administrator must master. Both tools are tested on the AZ-104 exam — expect command-completion questions, JMESPath query fills, and scripting-pattern scenarios. Attendees should leave able to write a re-runnable script and filter CLI output to a clean, pipeable list.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Azure CLI — Command Anatomy

# az  <group>  [<subgroup>]  <verb>  --parameters  --output <format>
az    vm        image          list   --location eastus  --output table

# Login and account management
az login                                         # interactive browser login
az account list --output table                   # list all subscriptions
az account set --subscription "<name-or-id>"    # set active subscription
📊

Output Formats

json (default) · table (human) · tsv (scripts) · yaml · jsonc (comments) · none

🔍

Built-in Help

az vm --help · az vm create --help · az find "create storage account"

⚙️

Persist Defaults

az config set core.output=table · az config set defaults.location=eastus

The CLI follows a consistent group → optional subgroup → verb hierarchy. Once you know the pattern you can guess most commands. The `--output tsv` format is the most scripting-friendly — no JSON parsing when piping to bash variables or loops. The `az find` AI assistant is useful during Cloud Shell lab tasks in the exam. The `az config set` defaults eliminate repetitive parameters across all commands in a session.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Filtering Output with JMESPath --query

# 1. Flat list — one VM name per line (ideal for bash loops)
az vm list --resource-group rg-prod \
  --query "[].name" --output tsv

# 2. Filter + rename columns for table display
az vm list --resource-group rg-prod \
  --query "[?location=='eastus'].{Name:name, Size:hardwareProfile.vmSize}" \
  --output table

# 3. Aggregate — count all VMs in the subscription
az vm list --query "length([])" --output tsv
🎯 Exam tip: [].name projects a flat scalar list — no brackets or quotes in TSV output. [].{Key:field} projects objects and renames columns. [?condition] filters. The exam tests filling in the --query value for a described output requirement.
JMESPath is the JSON query language built into the Azure CLI. The three patterns here cover the vast majority of exam scenarios: flat array projection, conditional filter with column renaming, and aggregate count. Emphasise the bracket distinction — `[]` projects every element, `[?condition]` filters. The `--output tsv` combined with a scalar projection is the canonical pattern for piping into shell scripts — no jq or sed required.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Az PowerShell — Module Patterns

# Connect and inspect context
Connect-AzAccount                                          # interactive login
Get-AzContext                                              # current sub + tenant
Set-AzContext -SubscriptionId "<subscription-id>"        # switch subscription

# Verb-AzNoun pattern
Get-AzVM -ResourceGroupName "rg-prod"                     # list VMs (read)
New-AzResourceGroup -Name "rg-dev" -Location "eastus"     # create (write)
Remove-AzResourceGroup -Name "rg-dev" -Force              # delete (destructive)

Standard Verb Set

  • Get-Az* — read / list resources
  • New-Az* — create resources
  • Set-Az* — replace configuration
  • Update-Az* — partial property update
  • Remove-Az* — delete resources

Object Pipeline Advantage

  • Returns .NET objects — no JSON parsing
  • Get-AzVM | Where-Object {$_.Location -eq 'eastus'}
  • | Select-Object Name, Location | Format-Table
  • Strongly typed properties — use dot-notation anywhere
Az PowerShell returns live .NET objects rather than JSON strings — its key scripting advantage for complex logic. You can dot-navigate nested properties and pipe through Where-Object without any string parsing. The Verb-AzNoun pattern maps directly to the CLI group-verb hierarchy, so knowledge transfers between tools. In automation contexts such as Azure DevOps or GitHub Actions, use a service principal or managed identity instead of Connect-AzAccount interactive login.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Idempotent Scripting & Choosing Your Tool

Check-Before-Create (CLI)

RG="rg-prod"
# Check existence before creating
if ! az group show --name "$RG" >/dev/null 2>&1; then
  az group create --name "$RG" --location eastus
fi

# PowerShell equivalent
if (-not (Get-AzResourceGroup -Name "rg-prod" `
          -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue)) {
    New-AzResourceGroup -Name "rg-prod" -Location "eastus"
}

When to Use Each Tool

  • ✅ CLI — Bash pipelines, Linux / macOS, quick Cloud Shell tasks
  • ✅ PowerShell — Windows environments, complex object logic, existing PS scripts
  • ✅ Either — both call the same Azure Resource Manager API underneath
  • 🎯 Exam: CLI questions use az syntax · PowerShell uses Verb-AzNoun
  • 🎯 Both are pre-installed in Azure Cloud Shell — no local setup required
🎯 Exam tip: az group create is idempotent by design — it will not error if the group already exists. Most other az * create commands will throw a conflict error — always check with az * show first or handle the exit code.
Idempotency is a recurring theme in AZ-104 exam questions on automation and scripting. A re-runnable script prevents duplicate resources and CI/CD pipeline failures. The `az group create` idempotency exception is a favourite exam gotcha — students often assume all create commands behave the same way. In PowerShell, the `-ErrorAction SilentlyContinue` pattern on Get-Az* is the idiomatic guard before New-Az*.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 02 · Scenario

A DevOps engineer is writing a bash script that must register every virtual machine in the rg-monitoring resource group with a third-party monitoring agent. The registration tool accepts VM names piped from standard input and requires exactly one name per line with no JSON brackets, quotes, or table headers. Which Azure CLI command produces the correct output?

A az vm list --resource-group rg-monitoring --query "[].name" --output json
B az vm list --resource-group rg-monitoring --query "[].name" --output tsv
C az vm list --resource-group rg-monitoring --query "[].{Name:name}" --output tsv
D az vm list --resource-group rg-monitoring --query "name" --output tsv
✓ B is correct — [].name projects the name field from every array element into a flat scalar list; --output tsv then renders each value on its own line with no brackets, quotes, or headers — exactly what the tool expects.
  • A — --output json wraps the result in a JSON array with square brackets and quoted strings, which would break the third-party tool's parser
  • C — [].{Name:name} projects an array of objects rather than scalars; TSV output of an object array includes the column header Name on the first line, which is not a valid VM name
  • D — querying "name" without the array wildcard applies the expression to the top-level array object itself, which has no name property and returns null
Give attendees 30–40 seconds to choose before revealing. Walk through each distractor: option A is tempting because JSON is the default and looks structured; option C trips up those who know object projection but forget it adds a header row in TSV mode; option D is a common beginner mistake — forgetting the array wildcard. Reinforce: scalar projection + TSV is the canonical bash-pipe pattern. The exam frequently tests this exact combination.
MODULE 00 · LESSON 03

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

ARM Templates & Bicep Fundamentals

Declare, parameterize, and deploy Azure infrastructure as code — then verify before committing changes.

  • Interpret ARM template structure: $schema, parameters, variables, resources, outputs
  • Read and write Bicep's declarative syntax; use modules and parameter files
  • Choose between incremental and complete deployment modes and understand their impact
  • Run deployments with az deployment group create and New-AzResourceGroupDeployment
  • Export existing resources, convert ARM JSON ↔ Bicep, and preview changes with --what-if
  • Package reusable templates as Template Specs
This lesson is the IaC foundation for the rest of AZ-104. Reinforce that ARM JSON and Bicep describe the SAME desired state — Bicep is just a cleaner authoring layer that compiles to ARM JSON. Administrators need both because the portal exports ARM JSON and many reference docs still use it.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

ARM Template Anatomy

Every ARM template is a JSON document with six top-level sections — only $schema, contentVersion, and resources are required.

📋

$schema + contentVersion

Declares template format version; content version is user-defined (e.g. "1.0.0.0"). Required on every template.

🔧

parameters

Inputs accepted at deploy time. Each parameter has a type, optional defaultValue, and allowedValues for validation.

📦

variables & resources

Variables hold reusable expressions. resources is the core — each entry has type, apiVersion, name, location, and properties.

📤

outputs

Values returned after deployment (e.g. connection strings, resource IDs). Consumed by downstream pipelines or nested templates.

🎯 Exam tip: the exam tests which sections are required vs optional — only $schema, contentVersion, and resources must be present.
Walk through a minimal ARM template on screen. Stress that apiVersion is per-resource-type and must match what the resource provider supports — a common deploy failure is using a stale apiVersion. The outputs section is heavily tested in pipeline-integration scenarios.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Bicep: Cleaner IaC, Same ARM Control Plane

Bicep is a domain-specific language (DSL) that compiles to ARM JSON — the ARM control plane never sees Bicep directly.

ARM JSON

"resources": [{
  "type": "Microsoft.Storage/storageAccounts",
  "apiVersion": "2023-01-01",
  "name": "[parameters('storageName')]",
  "location": "[parameters('location')]",
  "sku": { "name": "Standard_LRS" },
  "kind": "StorageV2"
}]

Bicep equivalent

param storageName string
param location string = resourceGroup().location

resource sa 'Microsoft.Storage/storageAccounts@2023-01-01' = {
  name: storageName
  location: location
  sku: { name: 'Standard_LRS' }
  kind: 'StorageV2'
}
  • Modules — split large templates into composable .bicep files; reference with module keyword
  • Parameter files — .bicepparam (Bicep-native) or legacy .parameters.json for per-environment values
  • Convert — az bicep decompile --file main.json → produces main.bicep (review output; complex templates may need manual fixes)
  • Build — az bicep build --file main.bicep → emits main.json for deployment or auditing
Emphasize that Bicep is the recommended IaC path for new AZ-104 work — it's what Microsoft Learn labs use. Decompile is imperfect on complex templates, so always review the output. Modules are the Bicep equivalent of linked/nested ARM templates.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Deployment Modes, Commands & Template Specs

Deployment Modes

  • ✅ Incremental (default) — adds/updates resources in the template; leaves unlisted resources untouched
  • ❌ Complete — enforces exact state; resources in the RG but not in the template are deleted
  • Pass --mode Complete (CLI) or -Mode Complete (PowerShell) to override
⚠️ Complete mode deletes resources silently — always run --what-if first in production.

Key Commands

  • az deployment group create --resource-group <rg> --template-file main.bicep --parameters @prod.bicepparam
  • New-AzResourceGroupDeployment -ResourceGroupName <rg> -TemplateFile main.bicep
  • What-if preview: append --what-if (CLI) or -WhatIf (PowerShell) — shows planned creates, modifies, and deletes without deploying
  • Template Specs: store versioned templates in Azure (az ts create); share across teams without file sharing
  • Export: az group export --resource-group <rg> → ARM JSON → decompile to Bicep
🎯 Exam tip: the exam distinguishes incremental vs complete mode impact — know that incremental never deletes, complete enforces declared state and removes everything else.
The what-if flag is a high-value exam topic — it's the safe way to preview Complete-mode impact before committing. Template Specs are tested as the recommended way to share reusable templates inside an organization, replacing the old pattern of storing ARM JSON in blob storage.

Module 00 · Prerequisites — Azure Administration Tools

Bicep → ARM → Deployment Pipeline

Walk through the forward path first — author Bicep, build to ARM JSON, deploy via CLI or PowerShell, ARM control plane validates and provisions. Then highlight the reverse path: export existing resources to ARM JSON, decompile back to Bicep for ongoing maintenance. The dashed what-if arrow shows you can preview the ARM control plane's plan without touching real resources.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 03 · Scenario

A team maintains a resource group named rg-webapp-prod that contains an App Service, a SQL database, and a storage account. A new Bicep template is written that declares only the App Service and SQL database. A junior engineer deploys it using az deployment group create --mode Complete. What happens to the storage account?

A The storage account is left unchanged because it was not referenced in the template
B The deployment fails with a validation error because the template is incomplete
C The storage account is deleted because Complete mode removes resources not declared in the template
D The storage account is moved to a system-managed resource group and preserved
✓ C is correct — Complete mode enforces the exact desired state declared in the template. Any resource present in the resource group but absent from the template is deleted by the ARM control plane after the declared resources are reconciled.
  • A — that describes Incremental mode (the default), which leaves unlisted resources untouched; Complete mode does not
  • B — ARM does not validate completeness of a resource group's contents; it only validates the template's own syntax and resource definitions
  • D — ARM has no "system-managed resource group" preservation mechanism; resources not in the template are simply deleted under Complete mode
Give attendees 30 seconds to read the scenario before revealing the answer. Emphasize the practical takeaway: always run --what-if before a Complete-mode deployment in production to see exactly which resources will be deleted. This scenario type — where a plausible-sounding distractor (option A) describes the wrong mode — is a signature AZ-104 question pattern.
LAB 01

LAB 00: Environment Setup

  • 🎯 Objective: Stand up a working admin toolchain — Cloud Shell, local CLI, and Bicep
  • 📋 Scenario: Contoso's operations team is onboarding new Azure administrators. Before any production work begins every admin must confirm their toolchain — Cloud Shell, Azure CLI, and Bicep — is installed, authenticated, and pointed at the correct subscription.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~15 minutes
Lab 01: LAB 00: Environment Setup. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 01 · Steps 1–3

Step-by-step

  1. In the Azure Portal click the Cloud Shell icon (>_) in the top navigation bar. On the 'Welcome to Azure Cloud Shell' dialog select Bash. On the 'You have no storage mounted' dialog choose your subscription and click Create storage — Azure provisions a backing storage account and file share automatically.
  2. In the Cloud Shell toolbar open the shell-selector dropdown (top-left, currently showing 'Bash') and switch to PowerShell. Confirm the prompt changes to 'PS /home/<username>>'. Switch back to Bash using the same dropdown — the prompt returns to '<username>@Azure:~$'.
  3. On your local workstation install the Azure CLI. Ubuntu/Debian: run the command below. Windows: winget install -e --id Microsoft.AzureCLI. macOS: brew install azure-cli. 
    curl -sL https://aka.ms/InstallAzureCLIDeb | sudo bash
Demonstrate steps 1 to 3. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 01 · Steps 4–5

Step-by-step

  1. Sign in to Azure from the local CLI. A browser window opens automatically; complete authentication with your Azure AD credentials. On headless or restricted systems append --use-device-code to receive a device-code login URL instead. 
    az login
    [ { "name": "<subscription-name>", "state": "Enabled", "tenantId": "<tenant-id>" } ]
  2. If the login output lists more than one subscription, set the intended lab subscription as the active context so all subsequent commands default to it. 
    az account set --subscription "<subscription-name-or-id>"
Demonstrate steps 4 to 5. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 01 · Steps 6–6

Step-by-step

  1. Install the Bicep CLI. Azure CLI downloads the latest Bicep binary and stores it alongside the CLI tools. 
    az bicep install
    Bicep CLI version 0.x.x (xxxxxxxxxx) installed successfully.
Demonstrate steps 6 to 6. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 01 · Steps 7–7

Step-by-step

  1. Confirm the active subscription, its state, and that it is flagged as the default. The IsDefault column must show True for your intended subscription. 
    az account show --query "{Subscription:name,State:state,IsDefault:isDefault,TenantId:tenantId}" --output table
    IsDefault State Subscription TenantId ----------- ------- -------------------- ------------------------------------ True Enabled <subscription-name> <tenant-id>
Demonstrate steps 7 to 7. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 01 · Validate

Prove It Worked

  • Confirm Azure CLI is installed locally and reports version 2.60 or later (first line of output).
    az --version
    azure-cli 2.6x.x
  • Confirm the Bicep CLI extension is installed and returns a version string.
    az bicep version
    Bicep CLI version 0.x.x (xxxxxxxxxx)
  • Confirm the active subscription is the intended lab subscription and IsDefault is True — a False or missing value means az account set was not run.
    az account show --query "{name:name,isDefault:isDefault,state:state}" --output table
    isDefault name state ----------- -------------------- ------- True <subscription-name> Enabled
Have everyone run the validation before moving on — this mirrors how the exam tests expected command output.
LAB 01 · Cleanup

Tear It Down

  1. If you created a Cloud Shell storage account solely for this lab and will not reuse Cloud Shell, delete the auto-provisioned resource group (default name: cloud-shell-storage-<region>). In the portal navigate to Resource Groups → cloud-shell-storage-<region> → Delete resource group, or run the command below. 
    az group delete --name "cloud-shell-storage-<region>" --yes --no-wait
  2. No teardown is required for the local Azure CLI or Bicep installation — both are prerequisites reused in every subsequent AZ-104 lab.
🔧 Lab tip: always clean up lab resources — idle public IPs, gateways, and VMs keep billing until deleted.
Walk through the teardown together so nobody leaves billable resources running.
LAB 01 · Done

What You Achieved

  • Stand up a working admin toolchain — Cloud Shell, local CLI, and Bicep
Confirm everyone completed Lab 01 before continuing.
LAB 02

LAB 03a: Manage Resources with Portal, PowerShell & CLI

  • 🎯 Objective: Create identical Azure resources three ways — Azure portal, Azure PowerShell, and Azure CLI — to build practical fluency with all three administration tools and understand when to reach for each.
  • 📋 Scenario: Your organization's Azure team uses the portal, PowerShell, and the CLI interchangeably across projects. To collaborate effectively and write automation scripts, you must demonstrate hands-on proficiency with all three toolchains by provisioning the same resource set each way.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~20 minutes
Lab 02: LAB 03a: Manage Resources with Portal, PowerShell & CLI. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 02 · Steps 1–3

Step-by-step

  1. Sign in to the Azure portal (https://portal.azure.com) and navigate to Resource groups → + Create. Enter the following values then click Review + create → Create: • Subscription: <your-subscription-name> • Resource group: az104-rg1 • Region: East US
  2. Inside az104-rg1, click + Create, search for 'Managed Disks', and select it. On the Create a managed disk Basics tab enter: • Disk name: az104-disk1 • Region: East US • Availability zone: No infrastructure redundancy required • Source type: None • Size: 32 GiB — Standard SSD (E4) Click Review + create → Create.
  3. Open Azure Cloud Shell from the portal toolbar (>_ icon) or navigate to https://shell.azure.com. When prompted, select PowerShell. If this is your first time, complete the storage-account setup wizard. In the PowerShell session, create resource group az104-rg2: 
    New-AzResourceGroup -Name 'az104-rg2' -Location 'eastus'
    ResourceGroupName : az104-rg2 Location : eastus ProvisioningState : Succeeded
Demonstrate steps 1 to 3. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 02 · Steps 4–4

Step-by-step

  1. Still in the PowerShell session, build a disk-configuration object and create a 32 GiB Standard SSD managed disk named az104-disk2 inside az104-rg2: 
    $cfg = New-AzDiskConfig -Location 'eastus' -CreateOption Empty -DiskSizeGB 32 -SkuName 'StandardSSD_LRS'
New-AzDisk -ResourceGroupName 'az104-rg2' -DiskName 'az104-disk2' -Disk $cfg
    Name : az104-disk2 ResourceGroupName : az104-rg2 Sku : StandardSSD_LRS DiskSizeGB : 32
Demonstrate steps 4 to 4. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 02 · Steps 5–5

Step-by-step

  1. Switch Cloud Shell to a Bash session by clicking the environment drop-down in the Cloud Shell toolbar and selecting Bash. Create resource group az104-rg3: 
    az group create --name az104-rg3 --location eastus
    { "name": "az104-rg3", "properties": { "provisioningState": "Succeeded" } }
Demonstrate steps 5 to 5. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 02 · Steps 6–6

Step-by-step

  1. Create a 32 GiB Standard SSD managed disk named az104-disk3 in az104-rg3: 
    az disk create --resource-group az104-rg3 --name az104-disk3 --sku StandardSSD_LRS --size-gb 32 --location eastus --output table
    Name ResourceGroup DiskSizeGb Sku ProvisioningState ----------- ------------- ---------- --------------- ----------------- az104-disk3 az104-rg3 32 StandardSSD_LRS Succeeded
Demonstrate steps 6 to 6. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 02 · Steps 7–7

Step-by-step

  1. Confirm all three resource groups are present by running the prefix-filtered query below. All three names should appear — this is the same command used in the Verify section, so you can run it at any point during the lab to check your progress: 
    az group list --query "[?starts_with(name,'az104-rg')].name" --output tsv
    az104-rg1 az104-rg2 az104-rg3
Demonstrate steps 7 to 7. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 02 · Validate

Prove It Worked

  • Verify all three az104-rg* resource groups exist and report ProvisioningState = Succeeded:
    az group list --query "[?starts_with(name,'az104-rg')].{Name:name,State:properties.provisioningState}" --output table
    Name State ---------- --------- az104-rg1 Succeeded az104-rg2 Succeeded az104-rg3 Succeeded
  • Verify all three managed disks exist with the correct SKU (StandardSSD_LRS) and size (32 GiB):
    az disk list --query "[?starts_with(name,'az104-disk')].{Name:name,RG:resourceGroup,SKU:sku.name,GiB:diskSizeGb}" --output table
    Name RG SKU GiB ----------- --------- --------------- --- az104-disk1 az104-rg1 StandardSSD_LRS 32 az104-disk2 az104-rg2 StandardSSD_LRS 32 az104-disk3 az104-rg3 StandardSSD_LRS 32
Have everyone run the validation before moving on — this mirrors how the exam tests expected command output.
LAB 02 · Cleanup

Tear It Down

  1. Delete all three resource groups in parallel using --no-wait so each deletion starts immediately without blocking the next command. The contained disks are deleted automatically as child resources: 
    az group delete --name az104-rg1 --yes --no-wait
az group delete --name az104-rg2 --yes --no-wait
az group delete --name az104-rg3 --yes --no-wait
  2. After 2-3 minutes, confirm deletion is complete — the query should return no output (empty result = all resource groups gone): 
    az group list --query "[?starts_with(name,'az104-rg')].name" --output tsv
🔧 Lab tip: always clean up lab resources — idle public IPs, gateways, and VMs keep billing until deleted.
Walk through the teardown together so nobody leaves billable resources running.
LAB 02 · Done

What You Achieved

  • Create identical Azure resources three ways — Azure portal, Azure PowerShell, and Azure CLI — to build practical fluency with all three administration tools and understand when to reach for each.
Confirm everyone completed Lab 02 before continuing.
Module 00 · Exam Simulator

Timed Knowledge Test

  • 4 exam-style questions covering this module
  • ⏱ Time limit: 8 minutes — the timer starts when you click Start
  • 🎯 Target score: 70% — review follows automatically
  • Navigate with ↓ / ↑ — change answers any time before the results slide
Module 00 exam simulator: 4 questions in 8 minutes. Encourage attendees to treat it like the real exam — no notes.
Question 1 of 4 — ARM Templates & Bicep Fundamentals — Deployment Modes

A company deploys Azure resources using an ARM template in the default deployment mode. The template includes a storage account and a virtual network. After the initial deployment, an administrator manually creates an additional network security group directly in the portal. The team then redeploys the same unmodified template without changing the deployment mode. What happens to the manually created network security group?

A The network security group is deleted because complete mode, which is the default, removes resources not defined in the template.
B The network security group is retained because incremental mode, the default, only adds or updates resources defined in the template and leaves unmanaged resources untouched.
C The deployment fails with a conflict error because ARM requires all existing resources in a resource group to be declared in the template before redeployment.
D The network security group is tagged as 'unmanaged' and quarantined until the next complete-mode deployment reconciles it.
Default = incremental (additive). Complete mode = destructive to unmanaged resources — the exam loves testing which mode deletes orphaned resources.
Question 2 of 4 — Azure Administrator Toolset — Tool Selection Decision Matrix

A platform engineering team must provision identical sets of Azure resources across 15 subscriptions in a repeatable, auditable, and version-controlled manner. The solution must enforce desired-state compliance and minimise configuration drift over time. Which Azure tooling approach best satisfies all of these requirements?

A Azure portal — the unified GUI supports multi-subscription views and provides audit logs via Azure Monitor Activity Log.
B Azure CLI Bash scripts — cross-platform and automatable, but scripts are imperative and require explicit idempotency guards to avoid duplicate resource creation.
C ARM templates or Bicep — declarative, idempotent, version-controllable definitions of desired state that can be deployed consistently across any number of subscriptions via deployment stacks or pipeline jobs.
D Azure PowerShell — rich object pipeline and Verb-AzNoun cmdlets simplify resource management, but imperative scripts do not inherently prevent drift without additional state-comparison logic.
Scenario asks for 'repeatable + version-controlled + drift prevention' → declarative templates (ARM/Bicep). Portal and imperative scripts answer 'quick one-off' scenarios.
Question 3 of 4 — Azure CLI & Azure PowerShell Essentials — Az PowerShell Cmdlet Patterns

An administrator opens a new PowerShell session and must manage resources in a subscription named 'Prod-EastUS' associated with their Microsoft Entra ID account. Their account has access to four subscriptions. Which command sequence correctly authenticates and sets the active subscription context using the current Az PowerShell module?

A Login-AzureAD; Set-AzContext -Subscription 'Prod-EastUS'
B Connect-AzAccount; Set-AzContext -Subscription 'Prod-EastUS'
C Connect-AzAccount; Select-AzSubscription -Name 'Prod-EastUS'
D Add-AzAccount; Set-AzContext -SubscriptionName 'Prod-EastUS'
Az module pattern: Connect-AzAccount → Get-AzContext (verify) → Set-AzContext -Subscription. Watch for AzureRM relics (Add-AzAccount, Login-AzureAD) as distractors.
Question 4 of 4 — Azure Administrator Toolset — Cloud Shell Behavior

A junior administrator launches Azure Cloud Shell from the Azure portal for the first time and is prompted to create a storage account. Several days later, after multiple browser restarts and session timeouts, they notice that custom scripts they uploaded are still present. They ask why files persist and what operational limit they must be aware of. Which statement is accurate?

A Cloud Shell mounts a persistent Azure Files share backed by the provisioned storage account, so uploaded files survive session termination; however, the shell container is recycled after 20 minutes of inactivity, terminating any running processes and in-memory variables.
B Cloud Shell stores files in an Azure Blob container that is preserved indefinitely; sessions never time out as long as the browser tab remains open.
C Cloud Shell persists all session state including environment variables and background processes by pausing the container rather than terminating it on disconnect.
D File persistence in Cloud Shell requires a Business or Enterprise Azure support plan; standard subscriptions receive only ephemeral session storage backed by Azure Table Storage.
Cloud Shell = persistent Azure Files share + ephemeral container (20-min inactivity timeout). Bash mode and PowerShell mode share the same mounted share at ~/clouddrive.
