Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 01 · Automating Deployment with ARM & Bicep

Define repeatable, version-controlled infrastructure using Azure Resource Manager templates and the Bicep domain-specific language.

  • Interpret ARM template structure and all six top-level properties
  • Parameterize Bicep templates with decorators for reusability across environments
  • Deploy templates via Azure CLI, PowerShell, and the portal
  • Export existing deployments and convert ARM JSON to Bicep
  • Use what-if preview to validate changes before applying them
This lesson covers Infrastructure as Code on Azure using ARM templates and Bicep. ARM is the native deployment layer for every Azure resource; Bicep is a first-class DSL that compiles to ARM JSON. The exam tests both CLI and PowerShell deployment commands, deployment modes, and what-if output interpretation. Expect scenario questions about choosing the right deployment mode and parameterizing templates for multi-environment CI/CD pipelines.

Module 03 · Compute

ARM Template Anatomy

{
  "$schema": "https://schema.management
    .azure.com/schemas/2019-04-01
    /deploymentTemplate.json#",
  "contentVersion": "1.0.0.0",
  "parameters": { },
  "variables": { },
  "resources": [ ],
  "outputs": { }
}
  • $schema — Required. URI identifying the template language version; drives IDE IntelliSense
  • contentVersion — Your semantic version string ("1.0.0.0"); informational only, not validated by ARM
  • parameters — Runtime inputs with type, defaultValue, and allowedValues
  • variables — Computed expressions evaluated once; reduce repetition in complex templates
  • resources — Array of resource objects — each needs type, apiVersion, name, and location
  • outputs — Values returned post-deployment (resource IDs, hostnames, connection strings)
🎯 Exam tip: resources is the only required array. Every resource entry must include apiVersion — it pins the ARM REST API feature set; an invalid or deprecated version causes an immediate deployment failure.
ARM templates are JSON documents submitted to the ARM API endpoint. Walk learners through the skeleton before opening a real template. Parameters come in from the caller; variables are evaluated internally — these are commonly confused on the exam. The apiVersion maps to the resource provider's REST API version, so different resource types carry different valid versions. Outputs are critical for chaining templates in pipelines — for example, passing a VNet resource ID into a downstream subnet deployment.

Module 03 · Compute

Bicep: Declarative Syntax, Modules & Decorators

@description('Target environment')
@allowed(['dev', 'staging', 'prod'])
param environment string = 'dev'

@minValue(1)
@maxValue(10)
param instanceCount int = 2

var storageName = 'sa${environment}${uniqueString(resourceGroup().id)}'

resource sa 'Microsoft.Storage/storageAccounts@2023-01-01' = {
  name: storageName
  location: resourceGroup().location
  sku: { name: 'Standard_LRS' }
  kind: 'StorageV2'
}

output storageId string = sa.id

Bicep vs ARM JSON

  • No $schema or contentVersion required
  • Decorators replace verbose allowedValues/minValue inline JSON
  • String interpolation with ${...} instead of concat()
  • Symbolic resource references — no resourceId() function needed
  • Bicep always transpiles to ARM JSON — deployed via identical az deployment commands
Modules split infrastructure into composable .bicep files — a parent passes parameters to children via the module keyword; the pattern used in Azure Verified Modules (AVM).
Bicep is a first-class Azure DSL — everything Bicep can express, ARM JSON can express. The decorators @description, @allowed, @minValue, @maxValue, @minLength, @maxLength, and @secure replace verbose inline JSON blocks. Modules are the Bicep equivalent of linked templates; they let you decompose large solutions into reusable files and are the standard pattern in Azure Verified Modules. For the exam, the key fact is that Bicep always compiles to ARM JSON before submission to the ARM API — the deployment commands are identical.

Module 03 · Compute

Deployment Modes & Commands

Incremental (default)

  • ✅ Adds or updates resources defined in the template
  • ✅ Resources not in the template are left unchanged
  • ✅ Safe for day-2 operations and partial updates
  • ❌ Can accumulate orphaned resources over time

Complete

  • ✅ Authoritative — resource group matches the template exactly
  • ✅ Removes resources present in the RG but absent from the template
  • ❌ Destructive — use with extreme caution in production
  • ❌ Requires all managed resources to be declared explicitly
# Azure CLI — deploy Bicep with environment-specific parameter file
az deployment group create \
  --resource-group rg-prod \
  --template-file main.bicep \
  --parameters @prod.bicepparam

# PowerShell equivalent
New-AzResourceGroupDeployment -ResourceGroupName rg-prod `
  -TemplateFile main.bicep -TemplateParameterFile prod.bicepparam
🎯 Exam tip: Incremental is the default — you must explicitly pass --mode Complete to enable resource deletion. Complete mode deletes anything in the RG not declared in the template, including resources added manually after the last deployment.
Deployment mode is a classic AZ-104 scenario question — the pattern is usually "a resource appears in the portal that was not deployed by the template; how do you ensure only template-defined resources exist?" The answer is Complete mode. Know both CLI and PowerShell syntax: the parameter file flag is --parameters in CLI and -TemplateParameterFile in PowerShell. ARM returns structured error codes and a Correlation ID on failure; use the Correlation ID with the Activity Log to trace the root cause.

Module 03 · Compute

What-if Preview, Template Specs & Export

🔍

What-if Preview

Shows creates, modifies, and deletes before committing — contacts ARM for an authoritative diff with no resources changed

📦

Template Specs

Store ARM/Bicep templates as versioned Azure resources — govern and share across teams using RBAC; referenced by resource ID at deploy time

⬇️

Export & Decompile

Export any resource group as ARM JSON from the portal or CLI, then convert to Bicep with az bicep decompile — decompiled output is a starting point, not production-ready

# Preview changes without deploying (colour-coded: green=create, yellow=modify, red=delete)
az deployment group what-if \
  --resource-group rg-prod --template-file main.bicep --parameters @prod.bicepparam

# Export resource group as ARM JSON, then decompile to Bicep
az group export --name rg-prod > exported.json
az bicep decompile --file exported.json   # produces exported.bicep
What-if is a mandatory pre-flight step in any mature deployment pipeline — it contacts the ARM API and returns a real change preview, not a local diff. Template specs store the template as a first-class Azure resource under a resource group; assign RBAC to control who can publish new versions and who can deploy. The export-and-decompile workflow bootstraps IaC from an environment built manually — the resulting Bicep usually needs cleanup: remove auto-generated resource names, add parameters, and consolidate hard-coded values into variables. The exam may ask which CLI command converts an ARM JSON file to Bicep syntax.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 01 · Scenario

A development team manages dev, staging, and production environments using a single Bicep template that deploys an App Service Plan and a web application. The SKU tier, minimum instance count, and allowed origin URLs differ per environment. The team must deploy each environment from a CI/CD pipeline without modifying the template file between runs. What should you configure?

A Maintain three Bicep template files (dev.bicep, staging.bicep, prod.bicep) with hardcoded values for each environment
B Create environment-specific .bicepparam parameter files and pass them at deploy time using --parameters @prod.bicepparam
C Add conditional expressions inside the Bicep template that switch SKU and instance values based on the value of an environment string parameter
D Publish the template to a template spec and create a separate spec version for each environment's configuration values
✓ B is correct — Environment-specific .bicepparam files decouple configuration from template logic. The same unmodified template deploys in every pipeline stage; only the parameter file changes, satisfying the no-template-modification requirement and following the principle of separating what to deploy from how it is configured.
  • A — Three separate template files duplicate all infrastructure logic; any structural change must be replicated across all three files, defeating the purpose of a single authoritative template
  • C — Embedding environment conditionals inside the template couples configuration to template logic; adding a new environment or changing values still requires editing and re-testing the template file
  • D — Template spec versions track changes to the template structure itself, not per-environment configuration values; creating a spec version per environment is not the intended use and produces an unmanageable version history
Give attendees 30 seconds to think before revealing the answer. The key teaching point is the separation of concerns: the .bicep file defines what infrastructure looks like; the .bicepparam file supplies how it is configured for a given environment. AZ-104 frequently pairs this with least-privilege scope and deployment mode questions in the same scenario. Reinforce that template specs solve organisational sharing and RBAC governance — not per-environment configuration variance.
Module 03 · Lesson 02

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 02 · Azure Virtual Machines

VM sizing families, managed disk tiers, encryption strategies, cross-scope mobility, and in-guest management tools.

  • Select appropriate VM size families and pricing tiers for workload requirements
  • Compare managed disk types on IOPS, throughput, and SLA guarantees
  • Implement SSE, Azure Disk Encryption, and encryption at host strategies
  • Move VMs across resource groups, subscriptions, and regions using the right tool
  • Configure VM extensions, boot diagnostics, and Run Command
This lesson maps to the AZ-104 "Deploy and manage Azure compute resources" domain — roughly 20–25% of exam questions. Disk encryption distinctions (ADE vs encryption at host for temp disk coverage) and the VM resize deallocation rule are the two highest-frequency exam topics here. Spend extra time on both before moving on.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

VM Size Families & Resizing

Size Families

  • B-series: Burstable — earns CPU credits at idle, spends on demand; ideal for dev/test and micro-services
  • D-series: General purpose — balanced CPU:memory (1:4); web apps, app servers, relational databases
  • E-series: Memory-optimized — high memory:CPU (1:8); SAP, large SQL Server, in-memory caches
  • F-series: Compute-optimized — high CPU:memory (1:2); batch processing, gaming servers, analytics
  • H-series: HPC — InfiniBand interconnect; simulations, CFD, financial risk modelling

Resize & Restart Rules

  • Resizing within the same cluster may complete with no downtime
  • Resizing to a size on a different cluster requires VM deallocation — not just a restart
  • A deallocated VM loses its non-static public IP and all temp disk contents

SKU Naming Convention

  • Standard_D4s_v5 → D-family · 4 vCPUs · Premium SSD capable · Generation 5
  • Key suffixes: s = Premium SSD support; d = local temp NVMe; a = AMD CPU
🎯 Exam tip: Stop ≠ Deallocate. A VM stopped from the OS still holds its cluster slot and incurs compute charges. Only deallocating via the portal or az vm deallocate releases the slot and halts billing — and is required before a cross-cluster resize.
The stop-vs-deallocate distinction is a classic trap. Candidates who stop a VM from inside the OS then try to resize will find the target size grayed out. Show the portal side-by-side: "Stopped (OS level)" vs "Stopped (Deallocated)" are different states. Also note that v5 generation SKUs are the current recommended baseline for new deployments.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Managed Disk Types

Type Max IOPS / disk Max Throughput Single-VM SLA Best For
Standard HDD 500 60 MB/s 95% Dev/test, backups, non-critical workloads
Standard SSD 6,000 750 MB/s 99.5% Web servers, lightly used enterprise apps
Premium SSD 20,000 900 MB/s 99.9% Production I/O-intensive, tier-1 databases
Ultra Disk 160,000 2,000 MB/s 99.9% SAP HANA, top-tier SQL, latency-critical HPC
🎯 Exam tip: The single-VM 99.9% SLA requires Premium SSD or Ultra Disk on ALL disks (OS + every data disk). Mixing in a single Standard HDD drops you to 95%. Ultra Disk constraints: must be in an Availability Zone, no snapshots, no disk export, and IOPS/throughput are billed independently of capacity.
The SLA column is the primary exam discriminator — students should be able to recite 95 / 99.5 / 99.9 without hesitation. Ultra Disk is uniquely configurable: you set IOPS and throughput independently and pay for what you configure, not what you consume. Briefly mention Premium SSD v2 as a newer option that bridges Premium SSD and Ultra with configurable performance and per-GB billing. Stress that mixing disk tiers locks you into the lowest-tier SLA.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Disk Encryption Strategies

🔑

SSE — PMK / CMK

Always-on server-side encryption in Azure Storage. PMK: Microsoft manages keys. CMK: your keys in Azure Key Vault. Does not cover the temp disk or host-level read/write caches.

🛡️

Azure Disk Encryption (ADE)

BitLocker (Windows) / DM-Crypt (Linux) inside the VM. Encrypts OS & data disk volumes. Requires Key Vault. Does not encrypt the temp disk or host caches. Cannot be combined with Encryption at Host.

🏗️

Encryption at Host

Hypervisor-layer encryption. Covers temp disk AND OS/data disk read/write caches. Data is encrypted before it leaves the physical host. Requires feature registration: Microsoft.Compute/EncryptionAtHost. Cannot be combined with ADE.

🎯 Exam tip: Only Encryption at Host encrypts the temporary disk and host caches. Pair it with SSE + CMK for a fully customer-controlled end-to-end chain — zero unencrypted data anywhere on the host, without installing anything inside the VM.
This is the single most-tested encryption concept in AZ-104. ADE is the intuitive answer when a question says "encrypt everything," but it misses the temp disk and host cache layer — that gap only closes with Encryption at Host. Remind students that ADE and Encryption at Host are mutually exclusive: choosing Encryption at Host means SSE handles OS and data disk encryption instead of BitLocker/DM-Crypt. SSE is always on by default; CMK just changes key ownership.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

VM Mobility & In-Guest Management

Moving VMs

  • Same subscription, different RG: Portal → Move Resources — no downtime, VM stays running
  • Cross-subscription: Same Move Resources operation; bring all dependent resources (NIC, disks, NSG, Key Vault) or update references
  • Cross-region: Use Azure Resource Mover (dependency-aware, validates feasibility) or the manual snapshot approach

Snapshot-Based Region Migration

  • Snapshot OS disk → copy to target region → create Managed Disk → deploy new VM
  • Allows simultaneous SKU change, AZ placement, and disk tier upgrade at migration time

VM Extensions

  • Small post-deployment agents installed and managed via the VM agent
  • Custom Script Extension: run scripts at deploy or on demand
  • Azure Monitor Agent: collect metrics, logs, and traces
  • DSC Extension: enforce desired state configuration
  • Managed via portal, CLI, ARM templates, or Bicep

Boot Diagnostics & Run Command

  • Boot diagnostics: captures serial console output + screenshot in managed storage; essential for diagnosing boot failures without RDP/SSH
  • Run Command: executes scripts via the VM agent — no open ports or network path required; invoke via portal or az vm run-command invoke
🎯 Exam tip: az vm run-command invoke routes through the Azure control plane, not the data plane — it works on VMs with no public IP, locked-down NSGs, or broken RDP/SSH configurations.
Cross-RG and cross-subscription moves are non-disruptive and often surprise administrators who expect downtime. Cross-region moves create a new VM from a snapshot — the original keeps running until you cut over, so plan DNS and IP changes carefully. Run Command is an increasingly common troubleshooting lifeline on the exam: if a question describes a locked-down VM that can't be reached over the network, Run Command is almost always the right answer over reimaging or redeploying.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 02 · Scenario

A financial services company must satisfy a compliance requirement that all data at rest on a Windows VM is encrypted — including the temporary disk and all disk read/write caches stored on the physical host. The security team wants the solution to require no agent or software installed inside the VM. What should you configure?

A Enable Azure Disk Encryption (ADE) with the EncryptFormatAll parameter set to true
B Enable Encryption at Host on the VM
C Configure Server-Side Encryption with a customer-managed key (CMK) stored in Azure Key Vault
D Enable both Azure Disk Encryption and Server-Side Encryption with a platform-managed key
✓ B is correct — Encryption at Host operates at the hypervisor layer, encrypting the VM's temporary disk and the read/write caches for all OS and data disks before any data is persisted on the physical host. It requires no software inside the VM — only subscription feature registration (Microsoft.Compute/EncryptionAtHost) and setting a VM property flag.
  • A — ADE installs BitLocker/DM-Crypt inside the VM and encrypts OS and data volumes, but it does not encrypt the host-level caches or the temporary disk at the hypervisor layer — the compliance gap remains
  • C — SSE-CMK encrypts data written to Azure managed disk storage, but the temporary disk and host read/write caches reside on local host storage that is outside the SSE boundary
  • D — ADE and Encryption at Host are mutually exclusive; they cannot be enabled on the same VM simultaneously, and even without that constraint, neither ADE nor SSE-PMK alone covers the temp disk and host caches
Give attendees 45 seconds before revealing the answer — this is precisely the scenario pattern the exam uses. The trigger words are "temporary disk" and "caches": whenever those appear together in an encryption question, the answer is Encryption at Host. Reinforce that ADE is a strong distractor precisely because it sounds comprehensive. Close by noting that Encryption at Host + SSE with CMK is the recommended pairing for organisations that need both host-level coverage and customer key control.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 03 · VM Availability & Scale

Design and deploy resilient, auto-scaling VM workloads using availability sets, zones, and Virtual Machine Scale Sets.

  • Design VM redundancy with availability sets and availability zones
  • Deploy and manage Virtual Machine Scale Sets — Uniform vs Flexible orchestration
  • Implement metric-based and schedule-based autoscale rules
  • Calculate composite SLAs and select the right resilience strategy for a workload
This lesson is one of the highest-weight areas in the AZ-104 compute domain. The three SLA tiers (99.9% / 99.95% / 99.99%) are a recurring exam topic, and the constraint that a VM cannot be moved into an availability set post-creation trips up many candidates. Close the lesson with the decision-table framework — exam questions almost always present a multi-requirement scenario and ask which pattern satisfies both availability and scale.

Module 03 · Compute Resources

Availability Sets vs Availability Zones

⚙️ Availability Sets — 99.95% SLA

  • Logical grouping within a single datacenter
  • Fault domains (FD): 2–3 separate power & network racks
  • Update domains (UD): up to 20 staggered maintenance windows
  • Protects against hardware failure & planned reboots
  • No incremental cost — pay only for the VMs
  • ❌ Cannot combine with availability zones on the same VM

🏢 Availability Zones — 99.99% SLA

  • 3 physically separate datacenters within one region
  • Independent power, cooling, and networking per zone
  • Protects against complete datacenter failure
  • ~2 ms inter-zone latency; small data transfer cost applies
  • Zone-redundant services span all three zones automatically
  • ✅ Required when SLA target is 99.99%
🎯 Exam tip: Single VM + Premium SSD = 99.9%  ·  Availability Set = 99.95%  ·  Availability Zones = 99.99%. You cannot add an existing VM to an availability set after creation — placement is fixed at deploy time.
Drill the three SLA numbers — they appear on almost every compute exam question. The critical differentiator is scope of protection: fault domains handle rack-level failures inside one datacenter; availability zones handle full datacenter outages. Note that not all Azure regions support availability zones, which can appear as a distractor. The mutual-exclusivity rule (sets vs zones) is also heavily tested.

Module 03 · Compute Resources

Virtual Machine Scale Sets

Manage a group of load-balanced VMs that scale automatically — the foundation for elastic, zone-resilient compute.

🔁

Uniform Orchestration

All instances share one VM image · Up to 1,000 instances (Marketplace image) · Built-in overprovisioning speeds scale-out · Legacy mode — still exam-tested

🧩

Flexible Orchestration

Mix VM types & configurations · Up to 1,000 instances · Native zone spread · ✅ Recommended for all new deployments

↔️

Scaling Operations

Scale-out: add instances  ·  Scale-in: remove instances  ·  Scale-up: resize SKU (requires deallocation)

🎯 Exam tip: Flexible orchestration is the strategic direction — prefer it for new deployments. Overprovisioning (Uniform) creates extra instances during scale-out and removes excess afterward, accelerating ramp-up at a brief cost spike.
Clarify the naming — both modes cap at 1,000 instances, but Flexible is what Microsoft recommends going forward. Overprovisioning is enabled by default on Uniform sets and is worth a sentence: Azure may create 110% of the requested count, then trim back, which is why initial cost can spike briefly. Scale-up requires deallocation, so it interrupts service unless you have multiple instances behind a load balancer — this distinction often appears in exam scenarios.

Module 03 · Compute Resources

Autoscale Rules

📈 Metric-Based Rules

  • Trigger on CPU %, memory %, disk I/O, or custom Azure Monitor metrics
  • Set an aggregation window — e.g. avg CPU > 75% for 5 min → add 2 instances
  • Define action: add/remove N instances, or set an exact target count
  • Cooldown period: default 5 min — prevents scale thrashing between actions
  • Scale-in protection: pin specific instances to survive scale-in events

🗓️ Schedule-Based Rules

  • Scale to a fixed count at a specific time or recurrence
  • Ideal for predictable load — business hours, overnight batch, weekly peaks
  • Set minimum, maximum, and default instance counts per window
  • Combines with metric rules — metrics override schedule during unexpected spikes
🎯 Exam tip: The cooldown period is the setting that prevents autoscale "flapping." If scale-in keeps firing immediately after scale-out, increase the cooldown. Scale-in protection is the setting for preserving VMs that are running long-running stateful jobs.
Walk through a realistic rule pair: "scale out by 2 when average CPU exceeds 75% for 10 minutes; scale in by 1 when CPU stays below 25% for 15 minutes; cooldown 5 minutes." Explain that the aggregation window and cooldown are independent — the window determines when the condition is truly met; the cooldown prevents re-evaluation too soon after an action. Emphasise that schedule and metric rules coexist — the exam tests whether candidates understand that metrics take precedence over scheduled minimums during a spike.

Module 03 · Compute Resources

Choosing Your Resilience Strategy

Pattern SLA Protects Against Best For
Single VM + Premium SSD 99.9% Host hardware maintenance Dev/test, non-critical workloads
Availability Set (≥2 VMs) 99.95% Rack failure, planned maintenance Fixed-count, same-DC redundancy
Availability Zones (≥2 zones) 99.99% Full datacenter failure Critical, fixed-size workloads
VMSS + Zones ✅ 99.99% Datacenter failure + demand spikes Elastic production workloads
❗ Gotcha: Availability sets and availability zones are mutually exclusive — you cannot apply both to the same VM. VMSS with Flexible orchestration spread across zones is the recommended pattern when you need both 99.99% availability and elastic scale.
This decision table is the core takeaway for the entire lesson — and the direct frame for the knowledge check. Reinforce the mutual-exclusivity rule: availability sets vs zones is a binary choice. VMSS + Flexible + zones is the preferred answer whenever the scenario requires datacenter-failure protection combined with auto-scaling. Cost note: availability sets have no incremental cost; availability zones add inter-zone data transfer charges (~$0.01/GB in most regions).

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

VM availability options

VM availability options: availability set (FD/UD) vs availability zones vs VMSS across zones with load balancer

Walk through this architecture left to right — name each Azure service and its role before moving on. This visual maps directly to exam scenario questions.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 03 · Scenario

A company is deploying a web application tier that must remain fully available if a single Azure datacenter fails. The deployment must also scale out automatically when the average CPU across all running instances exceeds 75% for five minutes. The solution must meet a 99.99% SLA. What should you deploy?

A Deploy VMs in an availability set and configure a CPU-based alert rule to trigger an Azure Automation runbook that provisions additional VMs when the threshold is breached
B Deploy a VMSS with Flexible orchestration spread across three availability zones and configure a CPU metric-based autoscale rule with a 75% threshold and a 5-minute aggregation window
C Deploy a VMSS with Uniform orchestration pinned to a single availability zone and configure a schedule-based autoscale rule for expected peak business hours
D Deploy individual VMs pinned to separate availability zones and apply a CPU-based autoscale rule to each VM independently via Azure Monitor
✓ B is correct — A Flexible VMSS spanning all three availability zones provides the 99.99% SLA by surviving a complete datacenter failure, while a CPU metric-based autoscale rule triggers automatic scale-out precisely when average CPU exceeds 75% for the defined window — satisfying both requirements.
  • A — Availability sets deliver only a 99.95% SLA (same-datacenter rack protection, not datacenter failure); Automation runbooks are not a native autoscale mechanism and lack built-in cooldown and flap prevention
  • C — A single availability zone provides no protection against full datacenter failure and cannot meet 99.99% SLA; schedule-based rules respond to time, not live CPU load, so unexpected spikes go unhandled
  • D — Autoscale rules are a VMSS-level feature and cannot be applied to individual VMs; there is no per-VM autoscale configuration in Azure
Give learners 30–45 seconds to read and select before revealing the answer. Walk through each distractor: A fails on SLA tier and mechanism; C fails on zone count and rule type; D fails because autoscale is a VMSS construct, not a per-VM setting. Emphasise the two-part requirement pattern — AZ-104 scenarios routinely combine an availability requirement with a scaling requirement, and the correct answer must satisfy both simultaneously.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 04 · App Service Plans & Scaling

Select the right plan tier, understand compute boundaries, and configure autoscale to balance performance and cost.

  • Select an appropriate App Service plan tier for workload requirements
  • Distinguish compute boundaries and resource sharing within a plan
  • Implement autoscale rules — Standard tier and above only
  • Optimize plan costs through app consolidation and off-hours scheduling
  • Understand per-app scaling limitations in Premium plans
This lesson is a consistent AZ-104 exam area — expect 2–3 questions on tier selection, especially around the Standard tier as the inflection point for deployment slots and autoscale. Real-world relevance: many organisations over-provision App Service plans, making cost optimisation immediately actionable for most attendees.

Module 03 · Compute

App Service Plan Tiers

🆓

Free / Shared

Shared infra · 60–240 min/day CPU · No SSL · No custom domains (Free) · Dev/test only

⚙️

Basic (B1–B3)

Dedicated VMs · Custom domains & SSL · Manual scale to 3 instances · No deployment slots · No autoscale

⭐

Standard (S1–S3)

Dedicated VMs · 5 deployment slots · Autoscale · Regional VNet integration · Hybrid connections

🚀

Premium v3 (P0v3–P3v3)

Higher-spec VMs · 20 slots · Autoscale to 30 instances · Private endpoints · Per-app scaling · Zone redundancy

🏭

Isolated v2 (I1v2–I3v2)

Dedicated App Service Environment · Private VNet injection · 20 slots · Max compliance isolation

Walk the tier ladder left to right — each step unlocks capabilities, not just raw compute. The most common exam trap is assuming Basic supports autoscale or deployment slots; it does not. Standard is the minimum for any production workload requiring blue-green deployments or dynamic scaling. Isolated uses an App Service Environment (ASE v3) — it's a separate resource with its own pricing model.

Module 03 · Compute

Feature Support by Tier

Feature Free Shared Basic Standard Premium v3 Isolated v2
Custom domains ❌ ✅ ✅ ✅ ✅ ✅
SSL / TLS bindings ❌ ❌ ✅ ✅ ✅ ✅
Deployment slots ❌ ❌ ❌ ✅ (5) ✅ (20) ✅ (20)
Autoscale ❌ ❌ ❌ ✅ ✅ ✅
VNet integration ❌ ❌ ❌ ✅ ✅ ✅ (ASE)
Per-app scaling ❌ ❌ ❌ ❌ ✅ ✅
🎯 Exam tip: Standard is the lowest tier that enables both deployment slots and autoscale — the AZ-104 exam tests this boundary repeatedly.
Walk the table column by column rather than row by row — the exam always frames questions as "which tier supports X?" not "what does Standard support?" Reinforce that Shared adds custom domains, Basic adds SSL and dedicated compute, and Standard is the true production-ready entry point. Per-app scaling is Premium-exclusive and allows capping instance count per individual app within the plan's ceiling.

Module 03 · Compute

Compute Sharing & Scale Mechanics

All apps assigned to the same plan share the same VM instances — the plan is the compute boundary, not the individual app.

graph LR subgraph Plan ["App Service Plan - Standard S2, 2 Instances"] AppA["App A"] --> VM1["Instance 1"] AppB["App B"] --> VM1 AppC["App C"] --> VM2["Instance 2"] end style Plan fill:#0B1120,stroke:#0078D4,color:#E2E8F0 style VM1 fill:#0078D4,color:#fff,stroke:none style VM2 fill:#0078D4,color:#fff,stroke:none style AppA fill:#1E293B,color:#94A3B8,stroke:#334155 style AppB fill:#1E293B,color:#94A3B8,stroke:#334155 style AppC fill:#1E293B,color:#94A3B8,stroke:#334155

Scale Up (Vertical)

  • Change tier — e.g. S1 → S2 → S3
  • More vCPU, RAM, and disk per instance
  • Brief app restart during the transition
  • Unlocks new features at higher tiers

Scale Out (Horizontal)

  • Add more VM instances to the plan
  • All apps in the plan scale together
  • Standard max: 10 · Premium v3 max: 30
  • Autoscale controls this dimension only
The shared-compute model is a critical architectural point — a noisy or memory-leaking app on a plan degrades every other app on the same plan. When students ask "how do I scale just one app?", the answer is per-app scaling in Premium v3, or move the app to its own dedicated plan. Emphasise that autoscale only controls scale-out (instance count); scale-up is always a manual portal or CLI action and triggers a brief restart.

Module 03 · Compute

Autoscale Rules & Cost Optimization

Autoscale (Standard+ only)

  • Metric-based: CPU %, memory, HTTP queue length, custom Azure Monitor metrics
  • Schedule-based: scale out Mon–Fri 08:00, scale in at 20:00
  • Always configure min / max / default instance counts — autoscale never exceeds the plan maximum
  • Scale-out cooldown: default 1 min · scale-in: default 5 min
  • When scale-in and scale-out rules conflict, scale-out wins

Cost Optimization

  • Consolidate apps: plan cost is per instance-hour — adding more apps to an existing plan is free
  • Off-hours schedule rules: scale to 1 instance overnight and on weekends
  • Right-size tier: don't pay for Standard if Basic compute is sufficient and slots are not needed
  • Reserved instances: 1-yr or 3-yr commitment saves ~35–55% vs pay-as-you-go
  • Delete unused plans immediately — an idle plan with zero apps still incurs hourly charges
🎯 Exam tip: An App Service plan is billed by instance count and tier — not by the number of apps hosted on it. Consolidation reduces cost without degrading availability.
Autoscale rule conflict resolution — scale-out winning over scale-in — is an occasional exam fact worth a quick mention. The cost consolidation point is highly actionable: moving ten apps from ten separate Basic plans onto one Standard plan with two instances dramatically reduces monthly spend and simultaneously adds slots and autoscale. Mention that Azure Advisor flags underutilised App Service plans and will surface consolidation recommendations automatically.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 04 · Scenario

A startup is deploying a production web application on Azure App Service. The engineering team requires deployment slots for zero-downtime staged releases and automatic scale-out based on CPU utilisation during peak hours. You must choose the lowest-cost plan tier that satisfies both requirements. Which App Service plan tier should you choose?

A Basic (B2)
B Standard (S1)
C Premium v3 (P1v3)
D Isolated v2 (I1v2)
✓ B is correct — Standard (S1) is the lowest tier that provides both deployment slots (up to 5) and metric-based autoscale. It satisfies all stated requirements at the minimum necessary cost.
  • A — Basic provides dedicated VMs, custom domains, and SSL, but does not support deployment slots or autoscale; it only allows manual scale-out to a maximum of 3 instances
  • C — Premium v3 supports both deployment slots (up to 20) and autoscale (up to 30 instances), but is significantly more expensive than Standard — it violates the lowest-cost requirement
  • D — Isolated v2 runs in a dedicated App Service Environment for maximum network isolation and compliance; it supports both features but is the highest-cost tier and far exceeds the minimum requirement
Give attendees 30–45 seconds to commit to an answer before revealing. The most tempting wrong pick is C — candidates who know Premium v3 "works" often overlook the lowest-cost constraint. Reinforce the exam pattern: when a question combines "lowest cost" with a feature requirement, identify the inflection tier — for deployment slots plus autoscale, that is always Standard. Basic is the most dangerous distractor because it sounds production-ready.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 05 · Configuring App Service

Operational configuration of App Service — slots, domains, certificates, backup, and network isolation.

  • Deploy code using deployment slots for zero-downtime releases
  • Map custom domains and validate ownership with CNAME, A record, and TXT verification
  • Implement TLS certificates and enforce minimum TLS version
  • Configure backup and restore strategies (Standard plan and above)
  • Secure app networking with VNet integration and private endpoints
This lesson covers the "day 2" operational tasks for App Service — the configuration work that happens after the initial deployment. These topics carry significant weight in the AZ-104 exam, particularly the directional distinction between VNet integration and private endpoints, and the sticky vs swapped slot settings pattern. Budget about 25 minutes for this lesson and encourage students to follow along in the portal.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Deployment Slots

Slot Basics

  • production — live, customer-facing; always exists
  • staging / preview / custom — named by you
  • Requires Standard plan or higher (Standard = 5 slots; Premium = 20)
  • Each slot has its own hostname: app-staging.azurewebsites.net
  • Swap is atomic — Azure warms up the target slot before flipping traffic
  • Auto-swap: promotes a slot to production automatically on successful deploy

Sticky vs Swapped Settings

  • ✅ Swapped (travel with the code): app settings and connection strings without the slot-sticky flag, handler mappings, always-on
  • ❌ Sticky (stay with the slot): any setting flagged as Deployment slot setting — e.g. a connection string pointing to a slot-specific database
  • Mark a setting sticky in portal: Configuration → Application settings → ☑ Deployment slot setting
🎯 Exam tip: The swap warm-up fires HTTP requests to the new slot before traffic switches — this eliminates cold-start downtime. Know which settings are sticky vs swapped; it is a favourite scenario question.
The sticky vs swapped distinction is the most-tested slot concept. A practical example: staging uses a dev database connection string marked sticky, so after a swap to production that string stays in staging and the production DB string stays in production. Auto-swap is ideal for CI/CD pipelines where every push to the staging branch should promote immediately; warn students that it bypasses manual validation gates.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Custom Domains & TLS Certificates

🌐

Custom Domain Mapping

CNAME — subdomain (www) → app.azurewebsites.net
A record — apex/naked domain → app's outbound IP
TXT record — ownership verification; Azure checks before accepting the binding
Both DNS record and TXT verification must exist before the portal accepts the domain

🔒

Certificate Sources

App Service managed (free) — auto-provisioned, auto-renewed; subdomains only, no apex support
Key Vault integration — bring your own cert with auto-rotation via access policy
Upload .pfx / .pem — manual upload; you own renewal

🛡️

TLS Bindings & Enforcement

SNI SSL — one certificate per hostname; no dedicated IP needed; modern standard
IP SSL — legacy; requires a dedicated inbound IP; extra cost
Minimum TLS version — enforce TLS 1.2 (or 1.3) under Configuration → General settings; historic default is TLS 1.0

🎯 Exam tip: The free managed certificate does not support apex domains. For a naked domain (contoso.com), use a Key Vault–backed or uploaded certificate bound with an A record.
Custom domain binding is a two-step process students often get wrong: first create the DNS records at the registrar, then add the domain in the portal — Azure validates the TXT record before accepting. Minimum TLS version enforcement is a common security governance requirement; the portal path and the default value (TLS 1.0) are both testable. SNI vs IP SSL is a cost and compatibility question — SNI is almost always correct for modern apps.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Backup & Restore

Requirements & What's Included

  • Requires Standard plan or higher — not available on Free, Shared, or Basic
  • Destination: Azure Storage account in the same subscription
  • Backup includes: app configuration, file content (/site/wwwroot), and linked databases (Azure SQL, MySQL, PostgreSQL)
  • Maximum backup size: 10 GB (app + DB combined)
  • Schedule: custom interval or on-demand; retention up to 365 days

Partial Backups & Restore

  • Partial backups — exclude large folders (e.g. /LogFiles, /Temp) to stay under the 10 GB cap
  • Restore targets: same app, a different deployment slot, or a different App Service entirely
  • Restore is destructive overwrite — no merge; current content is replaced
  • Database restore is optional — skip it if the DB is managed and restored separately
🎯 Exam tip: Backups (Standard+, manual/scheduled) vs snapshots (Premium only, automatic, 30-day retention) — the exam distinguishes which feature is available at which plan tier.
The most common student mistake here is assuming backup is available on all plans. Drill: Standard+ for backup, Premium+ for snapshots. The 10 GB combined limit catches teams with large media uploads — partial backups are the fix, not upgrading the plan. Also note that you must grant the App Service access to the storage account before scheduling backups; SAS token expiry will silently break scheduled jobs.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

App Service Networking

➡️

VNet Integration — Outbound

Lets the app initiate calls into a VNet — internal databases, VMs, private APIs. Requires a delegated subnet in the target VNet. Does not block inbound internet traffic to the app. Available on Standard plan and above.

🔐

Private Endpoint — Inbound

Assigns a private IP to the app inside a VNet subnet. Public network access can be fully disabled. DNS resolves app.azurewebsites.net to the private IP via a Private DNS Zone. True network-level isolation for inbound traffic.

🚦

Access Restrictions

IP-based allow/deny rules applied to the public endpoint. Useful for limiting access to known CIDRs or service tags (e.g. allow only Azure Front Door). The public endpoint remains active — traffic is filtered, not eliminated.

🎯 Exam tip: VNet integration = outbound (app → VNet). Private endpoint = inbound (VNet → app, no public IP). Access restrictions = filter the public endpoint. These three are the most confused networking concepts on the exam.
Map this on a whiteboard if possible: draw the app in the middle, an arrow pointing right for VNet integration (the app calls out), and an arrow pointing left ending at a private IP for private endpoint (callers reach in privately). Combining both features gives fully private bidirectional connectivity. Access restrictions are a lighter control and should not be used when true public-endpoint removal is required by policy.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 05 · Scenario

A company hosts a production web application on Azure App Service (Standard plan). The security team requires that the application must be reachable only from within the company's Azure VNet — no traffic from the public internet must be able to reach the app, even if a firewall rule is misconfigured or accidentally deleted. What should you configure?

A Enable VNet integration on the App Service and configure a delegated subnet in the target VNet
B Add an access restriction rule that allows only the VNet address space and sets the default action to Deny
C Create a private endpoint for the App Service and disable public network access on the app
D Migrate the app to an App Service Environment v3 on an Isolated v2 plan
✓ C is correct — A private endpoint assigns a private IP inside the VNet; disabling public network access removes the public endpoint entirely, so no internet path exists regardless of rule changes or misconfigurations.
  • A — VNet integration controls outbound traffic from the app to the VNet; it does not affect or remove the inbound public endpoint
  • B — Access restrictions filter the public endpoint but do not eliminate it; a deleted or misconfigured rule could still expose the app, which the scenario explicitly prohibits
  • D — ASE v3 does provide full network isolation, but it requires a dedicated Isolated v2 plan, a complete environment redeployment, and significantly higher cost — not the appropriate solution for securing an existing Standard-plan app
Give attendees 30–45 seconds before revealing. Emphasise the two-step requirement: creating a private endpoint alone does not automatically disable the public endpoint — you must also toggle "Disable public network access" in the portal. This two-step pattern is a reliable exam question. Also reinforce why ASE, while technically valid, is the wrong answer given the constraints: cost, complexity, and the fact that the app already exists on a Standard plan.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Lesson 06 · Containers — ACR, ACI & Container Apps

Build a private image registry, run isolated container workloads, and configure event-driven applications that scale to zero between bursts.

  • Build and push images to Azure Container Registry — tiers, tasks, and authentication
  • Deploy container groups to Azure Container Instances with appropriate restart policies
  • Configure Azure Container Apps with revision-based deployments and ingress
  • Implement scale-to-zero using KEDA triggers for event-driven workloads
  • Select the correct container service (ACI · Container Apps · AKS) for a given scenario
This lesson covers three Azure container services at increasing abstraction levels: ACR for storing images, ACI for fast isolated runs, and Container Apps for event-driven autoscaling. The AZ-104 exam tests all three and heavily weights service-selection scenarios — knowing when NOT to use AKS is as important as knowing when to use Container Apps. Estimated lesson time is 25 minutes.

Module 03 · Compute

Azure Container Registry — Tiers & Authentication

SKU Tiers

  • Basic — 10 GB storage; dev/test only; no geo-replication
  • Standard — 100 GB; webhooks; managed identity; zone redundancy
  • Premium — 500 GB; geo-replication; private endpoint; content trust; dedicated throughput
🎯 Exam tip: geo-replication and private link are Premium-only. The exam will describe a multi-region registry and expect you to select Premium.

ACR Tasks

  • Cloud-based build — no local Docker daemon required
  • Triggers: git commit, base image update, or cron schedule
  • az acr build --registry <name> --image app:v1 .

Authentication Methods

  • ✅ Managed identity — preferred for Azure-hosted workloads; zero credentials stored or rotated
  • ✅ Entra service principal — CI/CD pipelines, external systems, cross-tenant pull
  • ❌ Admin account — single username/password; legacy mechanism; disable in production
🎯 Exam tip: if the scenario involves an ACI or Container App pulling an image from ACR, the answer is almost always managed identity — it avoids secret management entirely.
ACR is a fully managed, OCI-compliant private registry. Differentiate tiers by geo-replication (Premium) and storage quotas. For authentication, steer candidates toward managed identity whenever the workload runs inside Azure — it removes the entire credential-rotation problem. ACR Tasks removes the local Docker dependency for builds, which is a common exam gotcha: you can build and push directly from source code without Docker installed on the agent.

Module 03 · Compute

Azure Container Instances

📦

Container Groups

One or more containers sharing a lifecycle, network namespace, and storage — analogous to a Kubernetes pod. Sidecar pattern: main app + log forwarder or reverse proxy on localhost.

🔄

Restart Policies

Always — long-running services; OnFailure — batch jobs that may fail; Never — one-shot tasks that must not restart

⚙️

Sizing & Billing

0.5–4 vCPU · 0.5–16 GB RAM per group. Billed per-second from container start to stop. Stops billing when the container group is deleted or stopped.

🎯 Exam tip: ACI provisions fixed resources — it does not autoscale. Use ACI for short-lived, isolated, or batch workloads; use Container Apps when elastic scaling is required.
ACI is the lowest-friction path to running a container in Azure — no cluster or orchestrator to manage. The sidecar pattern inside a container group is a frequent exam scenario: two containers sharing localhost for log shipping or TLS termination. Match restart policies to workload type: a nightly ETL job that should retry on error maps to OnFailure; a one-time migration script maps to Never. Emphasize that ACI has no native scale-out — this is the key differentiator versus Container Apps.

Module 03 · Compute

Azure Container Apps — Revisions, Ingress & Scale-to-Zero

Revision Model

  • Each deployment creates an immutable revision snapshot
  • Split traffic across revisions → blue/green & A/B testing
  • Deactivate old revisions without deleting the app or its history

Ingress Configuration

  • External — public HTTPS endpoint with automatic TLS certificate
  • Internal — accessible only within the Container Apps environment VNet
  • Disabled — background workers and queue processors (no inbound traffic)

Scale-to-Zero (KEDA)

  • Set minReplicas: 0 → zero compute cost when idle
  • Scale triggers: HTTP concurrency, Azure Storage Queue depth, Service Bus message count, cron schedule, custom metrics
  • Scales 0 → N as load arrives; returns to 0 when queue or traffic drains

Dapr Integration

  • Enable per-app Dapr sidecar for pub/sub, service invocation, and secret stores
  • Awareness only for AZ-104 — not deeply tested
🎯 Exam tip: minReplicas: 0 is the key setting for cost-zero idle periods — the default is 1, which keeps one replica running continuously.
Container Apps abstracts Kubernetes entirely — no nodes, no control plane to manage. The revision model makes zero-downtime deployments declarative: set a traffic weight percentage per revision. KEDA triggers are configured through the Azure portal or ARM/Bicep scale rules — you don't install KEDA directly. The ingress disabled mode is often overlooked: a queue processor that never receives inbound HTTP calls should have ingress disabled to reduce attack surface and simplify the network configuration.

Module 03 · Compute

Choose the Right Container Service

Azure Container Instances Azure Container Apps Azure Kubernetes Service
Autoscale ❌ Fixed vCPU/RAM ✅ 0 → N via KEDA ✅ HPA/KEDA — manual config
Scale to zero ❌ Not supported ✅ Native (minReplicas: 0) ⚠️ Requires Virtual Nodes
Cluster management None None (fully managed) Full control — node pools, CNI, etc.
Best for Batch jobs, sidecars, one-shot tasks Event-driven workers, microservices, APIs Complex orchestration, stateful sets, Helm
Pricing model Per-second, fixed allocation Per-replica-second + request-based Node VM cost + management fee
🎯 Exam tip: ACI = fast, fixed, isolated. Container Apps = elastic, event-driven, zero-ops. AKS = full Kubernetes when customisation demands it.
This comparison is the most-tested concept in this lesson. The exam constructs scenarios that push toward one service — 'a nightly batch job' maps to ACI with OnFailure restart; 'a microservice that must scale to zero between queue bursts' maps to Container Apps with KEDA; 'an existing Kubernetes workload with custom networking and stateful sets' maps to AKS. Train candidates to read for the scale-to-zero and cluster-management signals first — those two dimensions eliminate two of the three options in most scenarios.

Module 03 · Deploy & Manage Azure Compute Resources

Container pipeline

Container pipeline: dev pushes image → ACR build/tasks → deploy targets: ACI (fixed) and Container Apps (KEDA autoscale 0..N)

Walk through this architecture left to right — name each Azure service and its role before moving on. This visual maps directly to exam scenario questions.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 06 · Scenario

A company runs a background image-processing worker that consumes messages from an Azure Storage Queue. Messages arrive in short bursts several times a day; the queue remains empty for hours between bursts. The engineering team wants compute costs to be zero during idle periods and the worker to scale out automatically when messages appear. What should you do?

A Deploy the worker as an Azure Container Instance with the OnFailure restart policy and a queue-depth alert
B Deploy the worker as an Azure Container App with minReplicas: 0 and a Storage Queue KEDA scale trigger
C Deploy the worker to Azure Kubernetes Service and configure the Horizontal Pod Autoscaler on CPU utilisation
D Deploy the worker as an Azure Container Instance and trigger new instances on a timer schedule via Azure Logic Apps
✓ B is correct — Azure Container Apps with minReplicas: 0 and a Storage Queue KEDA trigger scales the worker from zero to N replicas as queue depth rises, then back to zero when the queue drains, eliminating all compute cost during idle periods.
  • A — ACI provisions fixed resources and has no autoscaling capability; the container group continues to incur cost regardless of queue depth, and an alert cannot trigger a scale-out
  • C — HPA scales on CPU/memory by default, not queue depth; AKS does not natively scale to zero and adds significant cluster management overhead for this workload
  • D — ACI still has no queue-aware autoscaling; a timer schedule over-provisions during quiet periods and cannot react to sudden bursts, leading to both wasted cost and delayed processing
Give attendees 30–45 seconds to choose before revealing the answer. Emphasise two key facts: minReplicas must be explicitly set to 0 (the default is 1, which keeps one replica running at all times), and the KEDA Storage Queue trigger polls approximate message count — scale-out begins as soon as the threshold is exceeded. This pattern is a high-frequency AZ-104 exam scenario — the 'scale to zero' and 'queue depth' signals together should immediately identify Container Apps as the answer.
LAB 07

LAB 03b: Deploy with ARM Templates & Bicep

  • 🎯 Objective: Export a portal-generated ARM template for a managed disk, parameterize and redeploy it via the Azure CLI, decompile it to Bicep, and validate the full infrastructure-as-code round-trip.
  • 📋 Scenario: Your team must provision managed disks repeatedly across dev, test, and prod subscriptions. You will capture a portal deployment as an ARM template, refactor it into a parameterized, reusable template, then convert it to Bicep — proving the modern IaC workflow end-to-end.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~30 minutes
Lab 07: LAB 03b: Deploy with ARM Templates & Bicep. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 07 · Steps 1–2

Step-by-step

  1. Create a dedicated resource group for all lab resources. Choose a region close to you and note the name — every subsequent command references it. 
    az group create --name <rg-name> --location <location>
    { "location": "<location>", "properties": { "provisioningState": "Succeeded" } }
  2. In the Azure portal, navigate to Home → Disks → + Create. On the Basics tab set: Resource group = <rg-name>, Disk name = lab-disk-01, Region = same region as the resource group, Disk SKU = Standard SSD. Accept all remaining defaults and click Review + create → Create. Once the deployment completes, click Go to resource, then open Home → Resource groups → <rg-name> → Deployments (under Settings) → select the most recent deployment entry. Click Export template, then Download to save azuredeploy.json and azuredeploy.parameters.json to your local working directory.
Demonstrate steps 1 to 2. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 07 · Steps 3–4

Step-by-step

  1. Open azuredeploy.json in your editor. Locate the 'parameters' object and add two new entries — 'diskName' (type string) and 'diskSizeGB' (type int, defaultValue 128). Then replace every hard-coded disk name value with [parameters('diskName')] and every hard-coded diskSizeGB integer with [parameters('diskSizeGB')]. Save the file. The edited parameters block should match the structure below.
    "parameters": { "diskName": { "type": "string" }, "diskSizeGB": { "type": "int", "defaultValue": 128 } }
  2. Redeploy the edited ARM template from the CLI, passing a new disk name and a smaller size as inline parameters. This creates lab-disk-02 entirely through code, without touching the portal. 
    az deployment group create --resource-group <rg-name> --template-file ./azuredeploy.json --parameters diskName=lab-disk-02 diskSizeGB=64
    "provisioningState": "Succeeded"
Demonstrate steps 3 to 4. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 07 · Steps 5–6

Step-by-step

  1. Install (or upgrade) the Bicep CLI extension if needed, then decompile the ARM JSON to a Bicep source file. The command writes azuredeploy.bicep in the same directory as the input file. 
    az bicep install && az bicep decompile --file ./azuredeploy.json
    WARNING: Decompilation is a best-effort process, as there is no guaranteed mapping from ARM JSON to Bicep.
  2. Open azuredeploy.bicep in your editor and review the conversion. Confirm that ARM template functions such as [parameters('diskName')] have been replaced by native Bicep param references, the $schema header is gone, and 'param diskName string' and 'param diskSizeGB int = 128' appear at the top of the file.
Demonstrate steps 5 to 6. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 07 · Steps 7–7

Step-by-step

  1. Deploy the Bicep file with a third distinct disk name and a larger size to prove the converted template is fully functional. 
    az deployment group create --resource-group <rg-name> --template-file ./azuredeploy.bicep --parameters diskName=lab-disk-03 diskSizeGB=256
    "provisioningState": "Succeeded"
Demonstrate steps 7 to 7. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 07 · Validate

Prove It Worked

  • List all managed disks in the resource group and confirm that lab-disk-01 (128 GB), lab-disk-02 (64 GB), and lab-disk-03 (256 GB) all show Succeeded provisioning state — one disk per deployment method.
    az disk list --resource-group <rg-name> --query "[].{Name:name, SizeGB:diskSizeGB, State:provisioningState}" --output table
    Name SizeGB State lab-disk-01 128 Succeeded lab-disk-02 64 Succeeded lab-disk-03 256 Succeeded
  • Verify the decompiled Bicep file exists locally and contains the correct param declarations, confirming a successful ARM-to-Bicep conversion.
    grep -E "^param disk" ./azuredeploy.bicep
    param diskName string param diskSizeGB int = 128
Have everyone run the validation before moving on — this mirrors how the exam tests expected command output.
LAB 07 · Cleanup

Tear It Down

  1. Delete the resource group and all managed disks inside it to stop incurring managed-disk storage charges. The --no-wait flag returns the prompt immediately while deletion runs in the background. 
    az group delete --name <rg-name> --yes --no-wait
  2. Remove the exported and decompiled template files from your local working directory. 
    rm -f ./azuredeploy.json ./azuredeploy.parameters.json ./azuredeploy.bicep
🔧 Lab tip: always clean up lab resources — idle public IPs, gateways, and VMs keep billing until deleted.
Walk through the teardown together so nobody leaves billable resources running.
LAB 07 · Done

What You Achieved

  • Export a portal-generated ARM template for a managed disk, parameterize and redeploy it via the Azure CLI, decompile it to Bicep, and validate the full infrastructure-as-code round-trip.
Confirm everyone completed Lab 07 before continuing.
LAB 08

LAB 08: Manage Virtual Machines

  • 🎯 Objective: Deploy a zone-resilient VM, resize it, attach and initialize a data disk, then build a Virtual Machine Scale Set with CPU-based autoscaling across all three Availability Zones.
  • 📋 Scenario: Contoso Ltd. is modernizing its web tier and requires high-availability compute that can resize on demand and scale automatically under load. You will provision az104-vm1 in Zone 1, reconfigure its size and storage, then deploy az104-vmss1 across Zones 1–3 with autoscale rules tied to CPU utilization.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~30 minutes
Lab 08: LAB 08: Manage Virtual Machines. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 08 · Steps 1–1

Step-by-step

  1. Create resource group az104-rg8 in East US 2 to contain all lab resources. 
    az group create --name az104-rg8 --location eastus2
    "provisioningState": "Succeeded"
Demonstrate steps 1 to 1. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 08 · Steps 2–2

Step-by-step

  1. Create VM az104-vm1 in Availability Zone 1 with size Standard_B2s and no public inbound ports. Portal path: Virtual Machines → Create → Azure virtual machine. Set: Resource group = az104-rg8, VM name = az104-vm1, Region = East US 2, Availability options = Availability zones, Zone = 1, Image = Windows Server 2022 Datacenter, Size = Standard_B2s, Public inbound ports = None. On the Networking tab set NIC NSG = None. 
    az vm create --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 --image Win2022Datacenter --size Standard_B2s --zone 1 --admin-username azureuser --admin-password <AdminPassword> --nsg-rule None --public-ip-address "" --location eastus2
    "powerState": "VM running" "zones": "1"
Demonstrate steps 2 to 2. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 08 · Steps 3–4

Step-by-step

  1. Resize az104-vm1 from Standard_B2s to Standard_B2ms. Note: resizing requires the VM to deallocate (brief restart). Portal path: az104-vm1 → Settings → Size → select Standard_B2ms → Resize. The portal warns that the VM will restart — acknowledge and proceed. Via CLI, deallocate first, then resize, then start. 
    az vm deallocate --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 && az vm resize --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 --size Standard_B2ms && az vm start --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1
    "vmSize": "Standard_B2ms"
  2. Add a new 32 GiB Premium SSD LRS managed data disk to az104-vm1 while it is running (hot-attach, no restart needed). Portal path: az104-vm1 → Settings → Disks → + Add data disk → Create and attach a new disk → Name = az104-vm1-datadisk01, Size = 32 GiB, Storage type = Premium SSD LRS → OK → Save. 
    az vm disk attach --resource-group az104-rg8 --vm-name az104-vm1 --name az104-vm1-datadisk01 --size-gb 32 --sku Premium_LRS --new
Demonstrate steps 3 to 4. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 08 · Steps 5–5

Step-by-step

  1. Initialize and format the new data disk from inside the VM using the Run Command extension. The script finds the RAW disk, initializes it with MBR, creates a maximum-size partition, and formats it NTFS with the label 'DataDisk'. The assigned drive letter (typically E:) appears in the output. 
    az vm run-command invoke --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 --command-id RunPowerShellScript --scripts "Get-Disk | Where-Object PartitionStyle -eq 'RAW' | Initialize-Disk -PartitionStyle MBR -PassThru | New-Partition -AssignDriveLetter -UseMaximumSize | Format-Volume -FileSystem NTFS -NewFileSystemLabel 'DataDisk' -Confirm:\$false"
    "message": "...DriveLetter : E\nFileSystemLabel : DataDisk\nFileSystem : NTFS"
Demonstrate steps 5 to 5. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 08 · Steps 6–7

Step-by-step

  1. Create Virtual Machine Scale Set az104-vmss1 spanning Availability Zones 1, 2, and 3 with a Standard SKU load balancer and 2 initial instances. Portal path: Virtual machine scale sets → Create. Set: Resource group = az104-rg8, Name = az104-vmss1, Region = East US 2, Availability zones = 1, 2, 3, Image = Windows Server 2022 Datacenter, Size = Standard_B2s, Initial instance count = 2. On the Networking tab: Load balancing options = Azure load balancer → Create a load balancer named az104-vmss1-lb. 
    az vmss create --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vmss1 --image Win2022Datacenter --vm-sku Standard_B2s --instance-count 2 --zones 1 2 3 --admin-username azureuser --admin-password <AdminPassword> --load-balancer az104-vmss1-lb --location eastus2 --nsg-rule None
    "provisioningState": "Succeeded" "sku": { "capacity": 2, "name": "Standard_B2s" }
  2. Create an autoscale setting for az104-vmss1 with minimum 2 instances, maximum 4 instances, and two rules: scale out by 1 when average CPU exceeds 70% over 5 minutes (cooldown 5 min), and scale in by 1 when average CPU drops below 30% over 5 minutes (cooldown 5 min). Portal path: az104-vmss1 → Settings → Scaling → Custom autoscale → Default profile → set min = 2, max = 4 → + Add a rule for each threshold. 
    az monitor autoscale create --resource-group az104-rg8 --resource az104-vmss1 --resource-type Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachineScaleSets --name az104-vmss1-autoscale --min-count 2 --max-count 4 --count 2 && az monitor autoscale rule create --resource-group az104-rg8 --autoscale-name az104-vmss1-autoscale --condition "Percentage CPU > 70 avg 5m" --scale out 1 --cooldown 5 && az monitor autoscale rule create --resource-group az104-rg8 --autoscale-name az104-vmss1-autoscale --condition "Percentage CPU < 30 avg 5m" --scale in 1 --cooldown 5
Demonstrate steps 6 to 7. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 08 · Validate

Prove It Worked

  • Confirm az104-vm1 is running in Zone 1 with the resized SKU Standard_B2ms.
    az vm show --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 --query "{size:hardwareProfile.vmSize,zone:zones[0],state:provisioningState}" -o table
    Size Zone State --------------- ------ --------- Standard_B2ms 1 Succeeded
  • Verify the 32 GiB data disk az104-vm1-datadisk01 is attached to az104-vm1 at LUN 0.
    az vm show --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vm1 --query "storageProfile.dataDisks[].{Name:name,SizeGB:diskSizeGb,Lun:lun}" -o table
    Name SizeGB Lun ----------------------- -------- ----- az104-vm1-datadisk01 32 0
  • Verify az104-vmss1 has 2 provisioned instances distributed across availability zones.
    az vmss list-instances --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vmss1 --query "[].{Instance:instanceId,Zone:zones[0],State:provisioningState}" -o table
    Instance Zone State ---------- ------ ---------- 0 1 Succeeded 1 2 Succeeded
  • Confirm the autoscale profile on az104-vmss1 shows min=2, max=4, and exactly 2 rules (scale-out at 70%, scale-in at 30%).
    az monitor autoscale show --resource-group az104-rg8 --name az104-vmss1-autoscale --query "{min:profiles[0].capacity.minimum,max:profiles[0].capacity.maximum,ruleCount:length(profiles[0].rules)}" -o json
    { "max": "4", "min": "2", "ruleCount": 2 }
Have everyone run the validation before moving on — this mirrors how the exam tests expected command output.
LAB 08 · Cleanup

Tear It Down

  1. Delete resource group az104-rg8 and all contained resources (az104-vm1, managed OS and data disks, az104-vmss1, az104-vmss1-lb load balancer, NICs, NSGs, public IPs, and autoscale settings). This operation is irreversible — confirm you no longer need the lab resources before running. 
    az group delete --name az104-rg8 --yes --no-wait
  2. Confirm deletion is in progress. The command returns 'Deleting' while active; it returns an error once the group no longer exists — both outcomes confirm the teardown is underway. 
    az group show --name az104-rg8 --query provisioningState -o tsv
🔧 Lab tip: always clean up lab resources — idle public IPs, gateways, and VMs keep billing until deleted.
Walk through the teardown together so nobody leaves billable resources running.

LAB 09

LAB 09a: Implement Web Apps

  • 🎯 Objective: Deploy an Azure App Service web app, configure a staging deployment slot, observe deployment-slot-sticky settings, perform a zero-downtime slot swap, and configure CPU-based autoscale on the App Service plan.
  • 📋 Scenario: Contoso's operations team needs a validated, zero-downtime release process for its customer portal. You will wire up an App Service staging slot workflow so new code can be tested before it reaches production, then add autoscale rules to handle traffic spikes.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~35 minutes
Lab 09: LAB 09a: Implement Web Apps. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 09 · Steps 1–1

Step-by-step

  1. Create a dedicated resource group for all lab resources. 
    az group create --name az104-rg9 --location eastus
    "provisioningState": "Succeeded"
Demonstrate steps 1 to 1. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 2–2

Step-by-step

  1. Create a Standard S1 App Service plan. Standard tier or higher is required to enable deployment slots. 
    az appservice plan create --name az104-asp9 --resource-group az104-rg9 --sku S1 --is-linux
    "status": "Ready"
Demonstrate steps 2 to 2. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 3–4

Step-by-step

  1. Create the production web app. Replace <unique> with 4-6 random alphanumeric characters to guarantee a globally unique hostname. 
    az webapp create --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --plan az104-asp9 --runtime "PHP:8.2"
    "defaultHostName": "az104-web<unique>.azurewebsites.net"
  2. Add a non-sticky application setting APP_VERSION=v1 to the production slot. Non-sticky settings travel with the code when slots are swapped. 
    az webapp config appsettings set --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --settings APP_VERSION=v1
Demonstrate steps 3 to 4. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 5–6

Step-by-step

  1. Add a sticky slot setting ENVIRONMENT=production to the production slot using --slot-settings. Slot settings are pinned to the slot and never cross to the other slot during a swap — this is the key behaviour to observe later. 
    az webapp config appsettings set --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot-settings ENVIRONMENT=production
  2. Create the staging deployment slot. In the portal you can also do this via App Services → az104-web<unique> → Deployment slots → Add Slot. 
    az webapp deployment slot create --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging
    "name": "staging"
Demonstrate steps 5 to 6. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 7–8

Step-by-step

  1. Add APP_VERSION=v2 (non-sticky) to the staging slot to represent the incoming release candidate. 
    az webapp config appsettings set --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging --settings APP_VERSION=v2
  2. Add a sticky setting ENVIRONMENT=staging to the staging slot. After the swap this value must remain on the staging slot, not follow the code to production. 
    az webapp config appsettings set --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging --slot-settings ENVIRONMENT=staging
Demonstrate steps 7 to 8. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 9–10

Step-by-step

  1. Build a minimal v1 PHP file, zip it, and deploy it to the production slot. 
    mkdir -p /tmp/v1app && printf '<html><body><h1>Version 1 - Production</h1></body></html>' > /tmp/v1app/index.php && zip -j /tmp/v1app.zip /tmp/v1app/index.php && az webapp deployment source config-zip --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --src /tmp/v1app.zip
  2. Build a v2 PHP file, zip it, and deploy it to the staging slot. 
    mkdir -p /tmp/v2app && printf '<html><body><h1>Version 2 - Staging candidate</h1></body></html>' > /tmp/v2app/index.php && zip -j /tmp/v2app.zip /tmp/v2app/index.php && az webapp deployment source config-zip --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging --src /tmp/v2app.zip
Demonstrate steps 9 to 10. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 11–12

Step-by-step

  1. Browse to the staging slot and confirm it serves the v2 page before swapping. The staging hostname pattern is https://az104-web<unique>-staging.azurewebsites.net. 
    az webapp browse --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging
  2. Perform the slot swap — staging to production. Azure pre-warms the staging instance and then atomically redirects traffic; no requests are dropped. In the portal: App Services → az104-web<unique> → Deployment slots → Swap. 
    az webapp deployment slot swap --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --slot staging --target-slot production
Demonstrate steps 11 to 12. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 13–13

Step-by-step

  1. Read the production app settings after the swap. APP_VERSION should now show v2 (the non-sticky setting moved with the code from staging) while ENVIRONMENT should still show production (the sticky slot setting never left this slot). 
    az webapp config appsettings list --name az104-web<unique> --resource-group az104-rg9 --query "[?name=='APP_VERSION'||name=='ENVIRONMENT'].{Name:name,Value:value}" --output table
    APP_VERSION v2 ENVIRONMENT production
Demonstrate steps 13 to 13. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 14–15

Step-by-step

  1. Create an autoscale profile on the App Service plan — default 1 instance, minimum 1, maximum 3. 
    az monitor autoscale create --resource-group az104-rg9 --resource az104-asp9 --resource-namespace Microsoft.Web --resource-type serverFarms --name az104-autoscale9 --min-count 1 --max-count 3 --count 1
    "enabled": true
  2. Add a scale-out rule: when average CPU exceeds 70% over a 5-minute window, add 1 instance. 
    az monitor autoscale rule create --resource-group az104-rg9 --autoscale-name az104-autoscale9 --condition "Percentage CPU > 70 avg 5m" --scale out 1
Demonstrate steps 14 to 15. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Steps 16–17

Step-by-step

  1. Add a scale-in rule: when average CPU drops below 30% over a 10-minute window, remove 1 instance. 
    az monitor autoscale rule create --resource-group az104-rg9 --autoscale-name az104-autoscale9 --condition "Percentage CPU < 30 avg 10m" --scale in 1
  2. Confirm autoscale is active in the portal. Navigate to App Service Plans → az104-asp9 → Scale out (App Service plan). The default profile should list min 1, max 3 with both rules visible under the Increase count and Decrease count headings.
Demonstrate steps 16 to 17. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 09 · Validate

    Running az104-web<unique>.azurewebsites.net
  • Confirm the staging slot exists and is also Running.
