LAB 14

LAB 06: Implement Traffic Management

  • 🎯 Objective: Deploy an internal Standard Load Balancer (az104-ilb) with a static private frontend IP and a Standard_v2 Application Gateway with path-based routing, then validate round-robin distribution and URL-pattern routing from a jump host.
  • 📋 Scenario: Contoso's app team requires resilient, path-aware routing for an internal web tier: an ILB for round-robin load distribution across two backend VMs, and an Application Gateway that forwards /images/* and /video/* traffic to dedicated backend pools.
  • ⏱ Estimated time: ~50 minutes
Lab 14: LAB 06: Implement Traffic Management. Walk through this live if possible; pause for everyone to catch up before moving on.
LAB 14 · Steps 1–2

Step-by-step

  1. Open Azure Cloud Shell (Bash) or a local terminal with Azure CLI ≥ 2.50. Set shell variables that all subsequent commands reference. Replace <location> with your nearest region, e.g. eastus or westeurope. 
    RG=az104-rg06
LOCATION=<location>
VNET=az104-vnet
  2. Create the resource group. 
    az group create --name $RG --location $LOCATION
    { "location": "<location>", "provisioningState": "Succeeded" }
Demonstrate steps 1 to 2. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 3–4

Step-by-step

  1. Create the virtual network (10.60.0.0/16) and the app-subnet (10.60.2.0/24) that will host the backend VMs. 
    az network vnet create \
  --resource-group $RG --name $VNET \
  --address-prefixes 10.60.0.0/16 \
  --subnet-name app-subnet \
  --subnet-prefixes 10.60.2.0/24
  2. Add jump-subnet (10.60.1.0/24) for the test VM and the appgw subnet (10.60.3.0/24). Application Gateway requires a dedicated subnet; no other resources may share it. 
    az network vnet subnet create -g $RG --vnet-name $VNET \
  --name jump-subnet --address-prefixes 10.60.1.0/24

az network vnet subnet create -g $RG --vnet-name $VNET \
  --name appgw --address-prefixes 10.60.3.0/24
Demonstrate steps 3 to 4. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 5–6

Step-by-step

  1. Create an NSG that permits inbound HTTP on port 80 and associate it with app-subnet. Standard-SKU load balancers block all traffic by default unless an NSG explicitly allows it on the subnet or NIC. 
    az network nsg create -g $RG --name app-nsg

az network nsg rule create -g $RG --nsg-name app-nsg \
  --name Allow-HTTP --priority 100 --protocol Tcp \
  --destination-port-range 80 --access Allow --direction Inbound

az network vnet subnet update -g $RG --vnet-name $VNET \
  --name app-subnet --network-security-group app-nsg
  2. Write a cloud-init YAML file to /tmp that installs nginx and creates stub pages under /images/ and /video/. The $(hostname) expression is intentionally unexpanded here (single-quoted heredoc); cloud-init evaluates it as a shell command at VM runtime so each response reveals which VM answered. 
    cat > /tmp/cloud-init-web.yaml << 'EOF'
#cloud-config
packages:
  - nginx
runcmd:
  - mkdir -p /var/www/html/images /var/www/html/video
  - echo "<h1>$(hostname) - images</h1>" > /var/www/html/images/index.html
  - echo "<h1>$(hostname) - video</h1>" > /var/www/html/video/index.html
  - echo "<h1>$(hostname)</h1>" > /var/www/html/index.html
  - systemctl enable nginx && systemctl start nginx
EOF
Demonstrate steps 5 to 6. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 7–8

Step-by-step

  1. Deploy VM1 into app-subnet with no public IP. --no-wait returns control immediately so VM2 can start provisioning in parallel. 
    az vm create -g $RG -n az104-vm1 \
  --image Ubuntu2204 --size Standard_B1s \
  --vnet-name $VNET --subnet app-subnet \
  --public-ip-address "" --nsg "" \
  --admin-username azureuser --generate-ssh-keys \
  --custom-data /tmp/cloud-init-web.yaml \
  --no-wait
  2. Deploy VM2 into app-subnet with no public IP. 
    az vm create -g $RG -n az104-vm2 \
  --image Ubuntu2204 --size Standard_B1s \
  --vnet-name $VNET --subnet app-subnet \
  --public-ip-address "" --nsg "" \
  --admin-username azureuser --generate-ssh-keys \
  --custom-data /tmp/cloud-init-web.yaml \
  --no-wait
Demonstrate steps 7 to 8. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 9–10

Step-by-step

  1. Deploy a jump VM in jump-subnet with a public IP. This VM will be the test client for both the ILB and the Application Gateway. Note the publicIpAddress in the output. 
    az vm create -g $RG -n az104-jumpvm \
  --image Ubuntu2204 --size Standard_B1s \
  --vnet-name $VNET --subnet jump-subnet \
  --admin-username azureuser --generate-ssh-keys
    { "publicIpAddress": "<jump-public-ip>", "privateIpAddress": "10.60.1.4" }
  2. Block until VM1 and VM2 finish provisioning before attaching them to the load balancer. 
    az vm wait -g $RG --name az104-vm1 --created
az vm wait -g $RG --name az104-vm2 --created
Demonstrate steps 9 to 10. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 11–12

Step-by-step

  1. Create the internal Standard Load Balancer az104-ilb. The --private-ip-address flag pins the frontend to 10.60.2.100 inside app-subnet; omitting --public-ip-address keeps the frontend entirely off the public internet. 
    az network lb create -g $RG \
  --name az104-ilb --sku Standard \
  --vnet-name $VNET --subnet app-subnet \
  --private-ip-address 10.60.2.100 \
  --frontend-ip-name ilb-frontend \
  --backend-pool-name ilb-backend
    "privateIPAddress": "10.60.2.100", "privateIPAllocationMethod": "Static"
  2. Create a TCP health probe on port 80. Then create a load-balancing rule that forwards traffic received on the frontend port 80 to backend port 80 and marks unhealthy members via the probe. 
    az network lb probe create -g $RG --lb-name az104-ilb \
  --name http-probe --protocol Tcp --port 80

az network lb rule create -g $RG --lb-name az104-ilb \
  --name http-rule --protocol Tcp \
  --frontend-port 80 --backend-port 80 \
  --frontend-ip-name ilb-frontend \
  --backend-pool-name ilb-backend \
  --probe-name http-probe
Demonstrate steps 11 to 12. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 13–14

Step-by-step

  1. Add both VM NICs to the ilb-backend pool. The query extracts each NIC's full resource ID; awk returns only the name (the final path segment). 
    NIC1=$(az vm show -g $RG -n az104-vm1 \
  --query "networkProfile.networkInterfaces[0].id" -o tsv | awk -F'/' '{print $NF}')
az network nic ip-config address-pool add \
  --resource-group $RG --nic-name $NIC1 \
  --ip-config-name ipconfig1 \
  --lb-name az104-ilb --address-pool ilb-backend

NIC2=$(az vm show -g $RG -n az104-vm2 \
  --query "networkProfile.networkInterfaces[0].id" -o tsv | awk -F'/' '{print $NF}')
az network nic ip-config address-pool add \
  --resource-group $RG --nic-name $NIC2 \
  --ip-config-name ipconfig1 \
  --lb-name az104-ilb --address-pool ilb-backend
  2. SSH to the jump VM and issue four curl requests to the ILB frontend IP. The hostname embedded in each response should alternate between az104-vm1 and az104-vm2, confirming round-robin distribution. If nginx is still initialising via cloud-init, wait 60 seconds and retry. 
    JUMP_IP=$(az vm show -g $RG -n az104-jumpvm -d --query publicIps -o tsv)
ssh -o StrictHostKeyChecking=no azureuser@$JUMP_IP \
  'curl -s http://10.60.2.100 && curl -s http://10.60.2.100 && curl -s http://10.60.2.100 && curl -s http://10.60.2.100'
    <h1>az104-vm1</h1> <h1>az104-vm2</h1> <h1>az104-vm1</h1> <h1>az104-vm2</h1>
Demonstrate steps 13 to 14. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 15–16

Step-by-step

  1. Create a Standard static public IP address for the Application Gateway frontend. 
    az network public-ip create -g $RG \
  --name appgw-pip --sku Standard --allocation-method Static
  2. Create the Application Gateway in Standard_v2 SKU inside the dedicated appgw subnet. Provisioning takes 10–15 minutes. The --servers flag seeds the default backend pool with VM1's private IP; a second named pool will be added next. 
    VM1_IP=$(az vm show -g $RG -n az104-vm1 -d --query privateIps -o tsv)

az network application-gateway create -g $RG \
  --name az104-appgw \
  --sku Standard_v2 --capacity 1 \
  --vnet-name $VNET --subnet appgw \
  --public-ip-address appgw-pip \
  --servers $VM1_IP \
  --http-settings-port 80 --http-settings-protocol Http \
  --frontend-port 80 --priority 100
    "operationalState": "Running", "provisioningState": "Succeeded"
Demonstrate steps 15 to 16. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 17–18

Step-by-step

  1. Create pool2, seeded with VM2's private IP. This backend pool will exclusively serve /video/* requests. 
    VM2_IP=$(az vm show -g $RG -n az104-vm2 -d --query privateIps -o tsv)

az network application-gateway address-pool create -g $RG \
  --gateway-name az104-appgw \
  --name pool2 --servers $VM2_IP
  2. Create the URL path map. The first rule (images-rule) routes /images/* to appGatewayBackendPool (VM1). The second rule (video-rule) routes /video/* to pool2 (VM2). All other paths fall back to appGatewayBackendPool. 
    az network application-gateway url-path-map create -g $RG \
  --gateway-name az104-appgw \
  --name url-path-map \
  --rule-name images-rule \
  --paths /images/* \
  --address-pool appGatewayBackendPool \
  --http-settings appGatewayBackendHttpSettings \
  --default-address-pool appGatewayBackendPool \
  --default-http-settings appGatewayBackendHttpSettings

az network application-gateway url-path-map rule create -g $RG \
  --gateway-name az104-appgw \
  --path-map-name url-path-map \
  --name video-rule \
  --paths /video/* \
  --address-pool pool2 \
  --http-settings appGatewayBackendHttpSettings
Demonstrate steps 17 to 18. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Steps 19–20

Step-by-step

  1. Switch the default request routing rule (rule1) from Basic to PathBasedRouting and bind it to the URL path map you just created. 
    az network application-gateway rule update -g $RG \
  --gateway-name az104-appgw \
  --name rule1 \
  --rule-type PathBasedRouting \
  --url-path-map url-path-map
  2. Retrieve the Application Gateway's public IP, then test path-based routing from the jump VM. /images/index.html should return VM1 content; /video/index.html should return VM2 content. 
    APPGW_IP=$(az network public-ip show -g $RG --name appgw-pip --query ipAddress -o tsv)
ssh -o StrictHostKeyChecking=no azureuser@$JUMP_IP \
  "curl -s http://$APPGW_IP/images/index.html && curl -s http://$APPGW_IP/video/index.html"
    <h1>az104-vm1 - images</h1> <h1>az104-vm2 - video</h1>
Demonstrate steps 19 to 20. 🔧 Lab tip: keep the Azure portal open alongside the slides.
LAB 14 · Validate

Prove It Worked

  • Confirm the ILB frontend IP is statically set to 10.60.2.100.
    az network lb frontend-ip show -g az104-rg06 --lb-name az104-ilb --name ilb-frontend --query privateIPAddress -o tsv
    10.60.2.100
  • Verify both VM NICs are registered in the ILB backend pool (expected count: 2).
    az network lb address-pool show -g az104-rg06 --lb-name az104-ilb --name ilb-backend --query 'length(backendIPConfigurations)' -o tsv
    2
  • Confirm the Application Gateway is in Running / Succeeded state.
    az network application-gateway show -g az104-rg06 --name az104-appgw --query '{state:operationalState,provisioning:provisioningState}' -o json
    { "provisioning": "Succeeded", "state": "Running" }
  • Verify the URL path map contains both routing rules: images-rule for /images/* and video-rule for /video/*.
    az network application-gateway url-path-map show -g az104-rg06 --gateway-name az104-appgw --name url-path-map --query 'pathRules[].{name:name,paths:paths}' -o table
    Name Paths ------------ ------------- images-rule ['/images/*'] video-rule ['/video/*']
Have everyone run the validation before moving on — this mirrors how the exam tests expected command output.
LAB 14 · Cleanup

Tear It Down

  1. Delete the resource group and every resource it contains — VMs, load balancer, Application Gateway, VNet, NSG, and public IPs. --no-wait returns immediately while deletion runs in the background. 
    az group delete --name az104-rg06 --yes --no-wait
  2. Remove the temporary cloud-init file from the local machine. 
    rm -f /tmp/cloud-init-web.yaml
🔧 Lab tip: always clean up lab resources — idle public IPs, gateways, and VMs keep billing until deleted.
Walk through the teardown together so nobody leaves billable resources running.
LAB 14 · Done

What You Achieved

  • Deploy an internal Standard Load Balancer (az104-ilb) with a static private frontend IP and a Standard_v2 Application Gateway with path-based routing, then validate round-robin distribution and URL-pattern routing from a jump host.
Confirm everyone completed Lab 14 before continuing.
Module 04 · Exam Simulator

Timed Knowledge Test

  • 7 exam-style questions covering this module
  • ⏱ Time limit: 11 minutes — the timer starts when you click Start
  • 🎯 Target score: 70% — review follows automatically
  • Navigate with ↓ / ↑ — change answers any time before the results slide
Module 04 exam simulator: 7 questions in 11 minutes. Encourage attendees to treat it like the real exam — no notes.
Question 1 of 7 — Virtual Networks, Subnets & IP Addressing

A network architect is sizing subnets for a new Azure deployment. The production workload requires 25 VM NICs in a single subnet, and the architect proposes using the range 10.10.1.0/27. Which statement correctly describes the usable host capacity of this subnet?

A The /27 subnet provides 32 usable addresses, comfortably accommodating 25 VMs.
B The /27 subnet provides 27 usable addresses, comfortably accommodating 25 VMs.
C The /27 subnet provides 30 usable addresses, comfortably accommodating 25 VMs.
D The /27 subnet is too small; a /26 is the minimum required to host 25 VMs.
Always subtract 5 (not 2) from the total address count for any Azure subnet — the three extra reservations beyond standard network/broadcast are for the default gateway and two Azure DNS relay addresses.
Question 2 of 7 — Network Security Groups

A company has an NSG attached to a subnet. The NSG contains three inbound rules: priority 100 allows TCP from 10.0.0.0/8 on port 443; priority 200 denies TCP from 10.1.0.0/16 on port 443; priority 65500 is the default DenyAllInbound rule. A VM inside the subnet receives a TCP connection on port 443 from source IP 10.1.5.10. What is the effective outcome?

A The traffic is allowed because priority 100 matches first and NSG evaluation stops at the first matching rule.
B The traffic is denied because priority 200 is more specific — 10.1.0.0/16 is a subset of 10.0.0.0/8 — and specificity overrides numeric priority.
C The traffic is allowed by the default AllowVnetInBound rule at priority 65000 after both explicit rules fail to match.
D The traffic is denied because the default DenyAllInbound rule at priority 65500 always overrides explicit Allow rules.
NSG evaluation is strictly first-match-wins by the lowest priority number — there is no prefix-length or specificity tiebreaker, and processing halts the instant any rule matches.
Question 3 of 7 — Network Security Groups & Application Security Groups

A company deploys web servers and database servers inside the same Azure subnet. The security team requires that only web servers can initiate connections to the database servers on TCP port 1433, without creating or maintaining individual per-IP NSG rules as the fleet scales up and down. The solution must use Azure-native networking features only. Which approach best satisfies this requirement?

A Create an Application Security Group (ASG) named asg-web for web servers and asg-db for database servers, assign each VM's NIC to the appropriate ASG, then write an NSG inbound rule referencing asg-web as source and asg-db as destination on port 1433.
B Configure a service endpoint for Microsoft.Sql on the subnet and restrict the database server to accept traffic only from that service endpoint.
C Create a separate subnet for database servers and attach a route table that drops packets not originating from the web-server subnet's prefix.
D Deploy Azure Firewall and write network rules using the static private IP of each web server as an allowed source for port 1433.
When a question says 'without maintaining per-IP rules as VMs scale,' the answer is Application Security Groups — they are the native mechanism for group-based NSG source/destination references.
Question 4 of 7 — Azure DNS & Name Resolution

An administrator must delegate the public subdomain app.contoso.com to Azure DNS so it is authoritatively resolvable on the internet. The parent zone contoso.com is hosted at an external domain registrar. Which sequence of Azure portal steps correctly completes the delegation?

A In the Azure portal, create a Public DNS zone named app.contoso.com → record the four Azure-assigned name server (NS) values shown on the zone overview → log in to the external registrar and add NS records for the app label inside the contoso.com zone pointing to those Azure name servers.
B In the Azure portal, create a Private DNS zone named app.contoso.com, link it to the production VNet, then inform the external registrar of the link ID.
C At the external registrar, replace the SOA record of contoso.com with Azure DNS name servers to transfer authority for the entire parent zone, then create an A record for app inside Azure DNS.
D In Azure DNS, add an Alias record set of type A inside contoso.com that points to the IP address of the app subdomain's front-end VM.
Subdomain delegation always requires NS records (not SOA or A/Alias) at the parent zone pointing to the child zone's name servers — Azure shows all four NS values on the zone Overview blade immediately after creation.
Question 5 of 7 — VNet Peering & Routing

A company has three Azure VNets in the East US region: VNet-Hub (10.0.0.0/16), VNet-Spoke1 (10.1.0.0/16), and VNet-Spoke2 (10.2.0.0/16). VNet-Hub is peered with VNet-Spoke1, and VNet-Hub is separately peered with VNet-Spoke2. No direct peering exists between the two spokes. A VM in VNet-Spoke1 attempts to communicate with a VM in VNet-Spoke2. What is the default behavior?

A Traffic flows from VNet-Spoke1 through VNet-Hub to VNet-Spoke2 because VNet peering supports transitive routing by default.
B Traffic fails because VNet peering is non-transitive; Spoke1 has no route to Spoke2's address space without a direct peering or a forwarding NVA.
C Traffic flows if the 'Allow forwarded traffic' option is enabled on both hub-to-spoke peerings.
D Traffic flows if 'Use remote gateways' is enabled on the Spoke1-to-Hub peering.
VNet peering is always non-transitive — the only native spoke-to-spoke solutions are a direct peering, an NVA in the hub with UDRs, or Azure Virtual WAN hub routing.
Question 6 of 7 — Secure VNet Access — Service & Private Endpoints

A company stores sensitive financial records in an Azure Storage account. The security team mandates two controls: all access must use a private IP address within the company's Azure VNet, and the storage account must have absolutely no public internet exposure. Which solution fully satisfies both requirements?

A Enable a service endpoint for Microsoft.Storage on the VNet subnet and add a VNet firewall rule to the storage account.
B Deploy a Private Endpoint for the storage account inside the VNet subnet and set the storage account's public network access to 'Disabled.'
C Configure Azure Firewall DNAT rules to intercept inbound VNet traffic and forward it to the storage account's public endpoint.
D Enable VNet integration on the storage account to bind it to the target subnet.
Service endpoint = traffic reaches the public endpoint over backbone (public IP still active); Private endpoint = private IP in your VNet + public endpoint can be fully disabled — choose Private Endpoint whenever 'no public exposure' is a hard requirement.
Question 7 of 7 — Load Balancing — Azure Load Balancer & Application Gateway

A company is launching a public-facing web application that requires HTTPS load distribution across a pool of web servers, SSL/TLS offloading at the load-balancing tier, cookie-based session persistence, and URL path-based routing — requests matching /api/* must route to an API backend pool while /images/* routes to a separate storage backend pool. Which Azure service is the correct choice?

A Azure Load Balancer Standard SKU
B Azure Application Gateway
C Azure Traffic Manager with the performance routing method
D Azure Virtual Network Gateway
Layer 7 requirements (SSL termination, cookie affinity, URL/path routing, WAF) always point to Application Gateway; pure Layer 4 TCP/UDP flow distribution points to Azure Load Balancer.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Lesson 01 · Azure Monitor — Metrics & Logs

Collect, route, and query telemetry from every Azure resource using Azure Monitor's unified data platform.

  • Distinguish metrics from logs and choose the right query capability for each
  • Configure diagnostic settings to enable resource log collection
  • Design a Log Analytics workspace for scalability and access control
  • Route data to Log Analytics, storage accounts, and Event Hubs
  • Understand the activity log's role as a control-plane audit trail and its export options
This lesson covers the foundation of Azure observability — the two data types (metrics and logs) and the plumbing (diagnostic settings, Log Analytics workspaces) that the AZ-104 exam tests heavily. Expect scenario questions about which destination to route data to and why. The distinction between metrics and logs, and when you need a diagnostic setting, is a high-frequency exam topic.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Metrics vs Logs — The Two Data Types

📈 Metrics

  • Numeric time-series values (CPU %, bytes/sec)
  • Collected every 60 seconds by default
  • Stored in Azure Monitor Metrics for 93 days
  • Supports aggregations: avg, min, max, sum, count
  • Split by dimension (e.g. VM name, API endpoint)
  • Visualise in Metrics Explorer; power alert rules

📋 Logs

  • Structured or semi-structured records with rich context
  • Queried with KQL in Log Analytics
  • Default retention: 30 days (configurable to 730 days)
  • Covers diagnostics, audit events, dependency traces
  • Types: Resource Logs, Activity Log, Entra ID Sign-in Logs
  • Support joins, aggregations, time-window analysis
🎯 Exam tip: Metrics are always on for free — no diagnostic setting needed. Logs require an explicit diagnostic setting to be collected and routed.
The single most common exam trap is assuming logs flow automatically. They do not — a diagnostic setting must be created per resource. Metrics Explorer is the go-to tool for numeric time-series; Log Analytics / KQL is for rich contextual log querying. Dimensions let you break down a metric like "total requests" by status code or server instance without running a query.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Diagnostic Settings — Enabling Resource Logs

A diagnostic setting is a per-resource configuration that specifies which log categories and metrics to collect and where to send them.

🗂

Log Analytics Workspace

Query with KQL; power dashboards, alerts, and Insights. Best for operational analysis.

📦

Storage Account

Long-term archive (compliance, audit). Blobs stored as JSON. Not queryable in-place.

⚡

Event Hubs

Stream to SIEM (Sentinel, Splunk) or real-time pipelines. Low-latency, high-throughput ingestion.

Each resource can have up to 5 diagnostic settings, sending different categories to different destinations simultaneously.
Walk through the portal path: resource → Monitoring → Diagnostic settings → Add diagnostic setting. Emphasise that each destination serves a distinct purpose: Log Analytics for querying, Storage for cheap long-term retention, Event Hubs for streaming to external systems. The "up to 5 settings per resource" limit is exam-testable. You can route the same category to multiple destinations at once.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Log Analytics Workspace Design

Workspace Topology

  • Centralised: one workspace per region → easier cross-resource KQL joins
  • Distributed: one workspace per environment (prod / dev) → isolation and cost split
  • Cost model: Pay-per-GB ingestion + retention; Commitment Tiers for >100 GB/day
  • Default retention 30 days free; extend to 730 days (charged after day 31)
  • Interactive retention vs Archive tier (up to 12 years, very low cost)

Access Control

  • Workspace-context: RBAC on the workspace — user sees all tables
  • Resource-context: user inherits access from the Azure resource — sees only that resource's logs
  • Built-in roles: Log Analytics Reader, Log Analytics Contributor
  • Table-level RBAC available for sensitive tables (SecurityEvent, etc.)
  • Microsoft Sentinel requires its own dedicated workspace
🎯 Exam tip: Resource-context access is the least-privilege default — users only query logs for resources they already have RBAC on.
Workspace design questions on the exam usually test centralised vs distributed trade-offs and the two access-control modes. Centralised simplifies cross-service KQL but can expose sensitive data — table-level RBAC or resource-context mode mitigates that. Commitment Tiers (100, 200, 300, 400, 500 GB/day) offer up to 30% discount over pay-per-GB. Archive tier is new-ish and appears in recent exam refreshes.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Activity Log — Control-Plane Audit Trail

The Activity Log records every write operation (Create, Update, Delete) performed against Azure resources at the subscription level — who did what and when.

🕵️

What it captures

ARM operations: deployments, role assignments, policy changes, VM start/stop/delete. Does NOT capture data-plane reads.

📅

Retention & Export

Native retention: 90 days (non-configurable). Export via diagnostic setting to Log Analytics, Storage, or Event Hubs for longer retention.

🔑

Schema in Log Analytics

Lands in the AzureActivity table. Query fields: Caller, OperationName, ResourceGroup, ActivityStatus, CorrelationId.

🎯 Exam tip: Activity log = control-plane (ARM). Resource logs = data-plane (e.g. Storage transactions, NSG flow). Know the distinction — they have different retention defaults and diagnostic-setting requirements.
The 90-day retention limit for the activity log is a high-frequency exam fact. Students often confuse the activity log (subscription-level ARM audit) with resource logs (per-resource data-plane diagnostics). The AzureActivity table in Log Analytics is often referenced in KQL scenario questions — know the key columns. Exporting to storage satisfies compliance requirements for >90 day audit retention.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Monitor data flow

Monitor data flow: resources/agents → metrics + logs → Log Analytics workspace → alerts → action group → email/SMS/webhook

Walk through this architecture left to right — name each Azure service and its role before moving on. This visual maps directly to exam scenario questions.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 01 · Scenario

A security team needs to run a single KQL query that correlates NSG flow log events with Azure Storage account transaction logs to investigate a suspected data exfiltration incident. Both resources are in the same subscription but currently have no diagnostic settings configured. What should you do?

A Enable Network Watcher for the subscription and query metrics from Metrics Explorer using dimension filters for both resources
B Create a diagnostic setting on the NSG to route NSG flow logs, and a diagnostic setting on the storage account to route transaction logs, both targeting the same Log Analytics workspace; then write a KQL join across the two tables
C Create a single diagnostic setting at the subscription level and route all resource logs to a storage account, then download and correlate the JSON blobs manually
D Route both resources' logs to an Event Hub and use Azure Stream Analytics to join the streams in real time
✓ B is correct — Diagnostic settings must be created per resource (one for the NSG, one for the storage account). Routing both to the same Log Analytics workspace is the prerequisite for a KQL join across tables such as AzureNetworkAnalytics_CL and StorageBlobLogs. This is the only option that enables a single interactive query correlating both datasets.
  • A — Metrics Explorer queries numeric time-series data (transactions per second, bytes) — it does not surface flow log records or support cross-resource correlation at the event level
  • C — A subscription-level diagnostic setting routes the Activity Log, not individual resource data-plane logs; storage account blobs are also not queryable with KQL
  • D — Event Hubs is designed for streaming to external SIEM or pipeline systems, not for interactive ad-hoc KQL joins; this adds unnecessary complexity and does not provide a unified query interface
Give attendees 45 seconds before revealing the answer — this is a multi-step reasoning question. The key insight is that Log Analytics is the only destination that enables KQL joins across tables from different resources. Reinforce that diagnostic settings are always per-resource, and that routing to the same workspace is what makes cross-resource correlation possible. Option C is a deliberate trap — subscription-level diagnostic settings only cover the Activity Log.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Lesson 02 · Alerts & Incident Response

Design and configure Azure Monitor alert rules, action groups, and alert processing rules to detect resource issues and control notifications at scale.

  • Create alert rules with scope, signal, condition, and action group
  • Compare metric, log search, and activity log alert types
  • Configure action groups for email, SMS, runbook, Function, webhook, and ITSM
  • Understand severity levels, alert states, and stateful vs stateless behaviour
  • Suppress alerts at scale using alert processing rules and maintenance windows
Lesson 02 focuses on Azure Monitor alerting — a topic that carries significant weight in the Monitor & Maintain domain on the AZ-104 exam. Emphasise the separation of concerns: alert rules define what fires; action groups define who and how to notify; alert processing rules intercept fired alerts to suppress or route them. Alert processing rules are a recent exam addition — expect at least one scenario question on maintenance-window suppression without touching individual rules.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Alert Rule Anatomy

🎯

Scope

The Azure resource(s) to monitor — a single VM, a resource group, a subscription, or a Log Analytics workspace

📡

Signal

The data stream evaluated: a metric time series, a log query result, or an activity log event stream

⚙️

Condition

The threshold or query that must be satisfied — e.g. CPU avg > 90 % over 5 min, or KQL returning rows > 0

🔔

Action Group

Reusable notification config shared across rules: email, SMS, push, voice, Logic App, Function, runbook, webhook, ITSM

🎯 Exam tip: action groups are reusable — one group referenced by many rules. Updating the group once changes notifications for all rules that use it. The exam tests this reuse pattern directly.
Walk through a concrete example: scope = resource group rg-prod, signal = Percentage CPU metric on vm-web-01, condition = average exceeds 90 % for 5 minutes, action group = email the ops-team distribution list. Reinforce that the action group is a separate ARM resource — it can be shared, versioned, and managed independently. A common exam distractor presents the action group as part of the alert rule itself; it is not.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Alert Types

📊

Metric Alerts

Near-real-time evaluation of a numeric time series. Configure aggregation (avg / max / min / total), operator (> < =), and granularity. Supports dynamic thresholds using ML-derived baselines.

🔍

Log Search Alerts

Runs a scheduled KQL query against a Log Analytics workspace or Application Insights. You define the time window (e.g. last 5 min) and the fire condition (row count, metric measurement). Evaluation latency: 1–5 min.

📋

Activity Log Alerts

Triggers on control-plane events: VM stopped, role assigned, policy changed. Includes Service Health alerts for Azure outages, planned maintenance, and security advisories. Near-real-time.

The exam distinguishes these three types by what they monitor. Metric alerts consume the Azure Monitor metrics pipeline — low latency, no query overhead. Log search alerts query the Kusto log store on a schedule. Activity log alerts react to ARM control-plane write events. A common exam question: 'which alert type notifies you when an Azure region is experiencing a service degradation?' — the answer is an Activity Log alert scoped to Service Health. Dynamic thresholds on metric alerts use machine learning and are useful when the baseline varies by time of day, e.g. CPU patterns that spike every Monday morning.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Severity, Alert States & Stateful Behaviour

Severity Levels

  • Sev 0 — Critical: Service down; immediate action required
  • Sev 1 — Error: Urgent degradation; page on-call engineer
  • Sev 2 — Warning: Needs attention within hours
  • Sev 3 — Informational: Awareness only; no immediate action
  • Sev 4 — Verbose: Diagnostic / debug-level signal
🎯 Exam tip: alert processing rules can filter by severity to route Sev 0–1 to on-call action groups and Sev 3–4 to ticketing queues only — without editing any alert rule.

Alert States

  • Fired: Condition exceeded — alert is active
  • Resolved: Condition cleared — auto-closed in stateful alerts
  • Acknowledged: Engineer has seen it (manual)
  • Closed: Manually marked complete

Stateful vs Stateless

  • Stateful: Fires once; auto-resolves when condition clears. No duplicate notifications. Default for metric alerts.
  • Stateless: Fires on every evaluation cycle the condition is true. Used when each log event is an independent incident.
Stateful vs stateless is a classic AZ-104 exam topic. Metric alerts are stateful by default: you receive one 'fired' notification and one 'resolved' notification per incident — not a flood every 5 minutes. Log search alerts can also be configured as stateful, which is the recommended setting for most production scenarios. Severity is a classification you assign in the rule — it does not inherently prevent action group execution; you need an alert processing rule to act on severity for routing decisions.

Module 05 · Monitor & Maintain Azure Resources

Alert Processing Rules

Applied after an alert fires — they intercept the fired alert and either suppress notifications or add/override action groups. Rules target a scope and can be time-limited with a recurring schedule for maintenance windows.

flowchart LR AR["Alert Rule\nFires"] --> APR{"Alert Processing\nRule Matches?"} APR -->|"Suppress action\nmaintenance window"| SUPPRESS["Notifications\nSuppressed"] APR -->|"No rule / add action"| AG["Action Group\nInvoked"] AG --> NOTIFY["Email · SMS · Push\nRunbook · Webhook · ITSM"] SUPPRESS -.->|"Alert still recorded\nin history"| HIST["Alert History\n(audit trail)"] style AR fill:#78350F,color:#FDE68A,stroke:none style APR fill:#1E293B,color:#94A3B8,stroke:#334155 style SUPPRESS fill:#374151,color:#9CA3AF,stroke:none style AG fill:#065F46,color:#6EE7B7,stroke:none style NOTIFY fill:#0078D4,color:#fff,stroke:none style HIST fill:#0F172A,stroke:#F59E0B,color:#E2E8F0
🎯 Exam tip: alert processing rules do not modify alert rules — the alert still fires and is recorded in alert history. Only the notifications are suppressed. Create one rule scoped to a resource group instead of editing dozens of individual alert rules.
This is the most exam-dense slide in the lesson. Alert processing rules sit between the fired alert and the action group — they are a separate ARM resource with their own scope, filter (subscription, RG, resource, severity, alert rule name), action (suppress or add action group), and optional recurrence schedule. The canonical maintenance-window scenario: one processing rule with a weekly recurring schedule (Saturday 22:00–02:00) scoped to rg-prod suppresses all 60 alert rules in that group. Fired alerts are still visible in Azure Monitor Alerts history for post-window review — nothing is deleted.

Knowledge Check

Lesson 02 · Scenario

A company has 60 alert rules configured across resources in their production resource group. Every Saturday between 10:00 PM and 02:00 AM, the infrastructure team applies OS patches. On-call engineers must not be paged during this window. Individual alert rules are owned by separate teams and must not be modified. What should you configure?

A Disable all 60 alert rules before the maintenance window begins and re-enable them afterward
B Create an alert processing rule with a Suppress notifications action, scoped to the production resource group, with a recurring weekly schedule for Saturday 10:00 PM – 02:00 AM
C Delete the shared action group linked to the production alert rules each Saturday and recreate it after the window
D Set the severity of all 60 alert rules to Sev 4 during the maintenance window so notifications are deprioritised
✓ B is correct — an alert processing rule with a Suppress notifications action and a recurring weekly schedule applies centrally to all alerts targeting the resource group scope, suppressing notifications for every matching rule during the window without modifying a single alert rule.
  • A — disabling 60 individual rules is exactly the operational overhead alert processing rules are designed to eliminate, and re-enabling them manually risks missing rules or leaving them off
  • C — deleting and recreating the action group destroys its configuration and breaks all alert rules that reference it, including those outside the maintenance window scope
  • D — severity is a classification label only; it does not suppress notifications or prevent action groups from triggering unless an alert processing rule explicitly filters on severity
Give attendees 45 seconds to select before revealing. The key discrimination is between suppressing a notification (alert processing rule — non-destructive, scoped, scheduled) versus suppressing the alert rule itself (requires modifying each rule). Reinforce that suppressed alerts are still recorded in Azure Monitor alert history — the patching team can review what fired during the window the next morning. This is the most commonly tested alert processing rule scenario on the AZ-104 exam.
